The Sophist Argument for Suspension of Judgment and Its Refutation
Razi presents the sophist argument and responses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the sophist argument that leads to suspension of judgment and how later scholars responded by defending self-evident truths.

Core Idea
Skepticism about first principles ends in self-defeat.

In this passage, Imam Razi presents the position of the Sophists, who argue that all knowledge sources—sense, imagination, and reason—are unreliable, leading to the suspension of judgment. However, he also records the responses that highlight the solidity of self-evident truths and the self-defeating nature of radical skepticism.

Guided Path
1
Read the sophist argument
Read the passage from the beginning to the point where they conclude 'there is no way except suspension of judgment.' Notice how they reason from the unreliability of sense, imagination, and reason to the need for a higher judge, and why inference fails.
The argument relies on the idea of circularity (دور).
2
Examine the first objection
The text says 'It is not said: This speech...' which is an objection pointing out a contradiction if the sophists claim knowledge, or admission of failure otherwise. Understand the force of this objection.
3
Study the marginal notes
Read the two marginal notes labeled (1) and (2). The first distinguishes between debatable approvals and solid truths acknowledged by all. The second argues that failure to answer does not let doubt persist because first principles are self-sufficient.
These notes provide the defense of self-evident truths.
4
Analyze the second objection and reply
Read the part starting 'It is not said in response to them...' and the sophist's reply. The objection says the sophist's own doubt is either self-evident or based on self-evidents, so it would impugn itself. The sophist replies they only aim to cast doubt, not to refute definitively.
This reveals the self-defeating nature of radical skepticism.
5
Reflect on the core lesson
Consider why skepticism about first principles cannot be coherently maintained. How does the passage show that the sophist position is self-undermining?
The core idea is that any argument against self-evident truths must use logic and reason, thus presupposing them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفرقة : الرابعة السوفسطائية ) الذين قدموا في الحسيات والبديهيات. وقالوا ظهر بكلام الفريقين تطرق التهمة إلى الحاكم الحسى والخيالي والعقلى ، فلا بد . وأن يكون في حاكم آخر فوقها ، ولا يجوز أن يكون ذلك الحاكم هو الاستدلال لأنه فرعها ، ولو معناهما به لزم الدور ولا نجد حاكما آخر فوقها ، وإذا لا طريق إلا التوقف .
•
لا يقال : هذا الكلام الذى ذكر تم إن أفا. ك علما بفساد الحسبات والبديهيات قد ناقضت وإلا قد اعترفت بسقوطه
.
(1) أقول أما استحسان الأشياء واستقباحها فيجى القول فيها ، وأما مقتضيات الطبائع والعادات والديانات فلا شك في كونها مؤثرة في اعتقادات الدوام لكنها لا تعارض متانة الحق الذي يعترف به جميع المقلاء حتى البله والصبيان والمجانين ، وقد حذر العلماء طالبي الحق عن متابعة الأهواء والطبائع والعادات مثل قول القائل رؤساء الشياطين ثلاثة شوائب الطبيعة ووساوس العادة ونواميس الأمثلة ، ولا شك أن البديهيات لا تقدح بها .
(٢) أقول : عدم الاشتغال بالجواب لا يقتضى بقاء الشبرة القادحة في الأوليات ، فإنها مع جزم العقل غير مؤثرة في العقول السليمة ، بل إنما لا يشتغل بالجواب لفقدان ما يتفقون عليه من مبادىء الإيجاب ولكون الأوليات مستغنية عن الذب عنها بالحجج والبينات .
لا يقال في جوابهم : إن شهتكم التي أوردة، وها ليست قضايا حسية فهي إما بديهيات وإما نظريات مستندة إلى بديهيات ، فلو كانت قادمة فى البديهيات لكانت قادحة في أنفسها .
لأنهم يقولون : نحن لم نقصد فى إيراد هذه الشبهة إبطال البديهيات باليقين بل قصدنا إيقاع الشك فيها وكيف ما كان مقصودنا حاصل .
The fourth group: the Sophists, who impugned sense-perceptions and self-evident truths. They said: It has become clear from the speech of the two parties that suspicion attaches to the sense-governor, the imaginative, and the rational. Therefore there must be another governor above them. It is not possible for that governor to be inference because it is a branch of them, and if [their meaning?] with it, circularity would follow. And we do not find another governor above them. Hence there is no way except suspension of judgment.
•
It is not said: This speech that you mentioned, if it [؟] knowledge of the corruption of sense-perceptions and self-evident truths, you have contradicted [yourself]; otherwise you have admitted its failure.
(1) I say: As for approval and disapproval of things, there is room for discussion about them. As for the exigencies of natures, habits, and religions, there is no doubt that they influence lasting beliefs, but they do not contradict the solidity of truth which all rational persons, even the foolish, children, and the insane, acknowledge. The scholars have warned seekers of truth against following desires, natures, and habits, as the saying goes: 'The leaders of devils are three: the impurities of nature, the whisperings of habit, and the laws of examples.' There is no doubt that self-evident truths are not impugned by them.
(2) I say: The failure to engage in answering does not necessitate the persistence of the impugning doubt about first principles, for with the judgment of reason they are not effective in sound minds. Rather, one does not engage in answering due to lack of agreed-upon principles of affirmation, and because first principles are independent of defense by proofs and evidences.
It is not said in response to them: Your doubt that you raised, it is not a sense-perception; it is either self-evident or theoretical based on self-evident truths. So if it were impugning self-evident truths, it would impugn themselves.
Because they say: We did not intend in raising this doubt to definitively refute self-evident truths, but rather to cast doubt upon them; however it may be, our purpose is achieved.
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage presents the Sophist school (al-sufasa'iyya), the fourth group attacking sense-perceptions and self-evident truths. They argue that since suspicion has been shown to attach to sense, imagination, and reason, there must be a higher judge above them. Inference cannot be that judge because it depends on them, leading to circularity. Since no such higher judge exists, the only option is suspension of judgment (tawaqquf). Then an objection is raised: if this speech of yours claims to know that sense and self-evidents are corrupt, you contradict yourselves; otherwise you admit its failure. The first marginal note responds that approval and disapproval are debatable, but exigencies of nature, habit, and religion do not contradict the truth acknowledged by all rational beings; scholars have warned against following these. The second marginal note says that not answering does not let the doubt persist, because self-evidents are not affected in sound minds; answering is omitted due to lack of common premises and because first principles need no defense. Then another objection: the doubt you raised is itself not a sense-perception; it is either self-evident or theoretical based on self-evidents, so if it impugns self-evidents, it would impugn itself. The sophists reply that their goal is not to definitively refute but to cast doubt, and they achieve that.

Key Terms
سوفسطائية
توقف
حاكم حسى
حاكم خيالي
حاكم عقلى
دور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Skepticism about self-evident truths is a valid position that does not lead to contradiction.
Correction: Any argument against self-evident truths must itself rely on self-evident truths or logic, leading to a self-defeating position.

Checkpoint
According to the Sophists, why can inference not serve as a higher judge?
A) Because it is not accessible to all people
B) Because inference is a branch of sense, imagination, and reason, leading to circularity
C) Because inference requires training
D) Because inference is unreliable

Key Takeaway
The Sophist attempt to suspend judgment fails because it undermines itself; self-evident truths withstand skeptical challenges.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you entertained doubts about the foundational truths of your faith—like Allah's existence or the truth of revelation—not because you had evidence against them, but because you wanted to avoid the responsibility of conviction? Will you abandon that comfortable suspension and embrace the self-evident truths that even children and the foolish acknowledge?
?
The Sophists aimed only to cast doubt, not to refute. Is there an area of your life where you hide behind 'I'm not sure' to avoid acting on what you know is true? What step will you take today to replace doubt with decisive action?
?
Razi warns that desires, habits, and imitations can influence beliefs. Which of these three—your own desires, cultural habits, or blind imitation—has most clouded your certainty about a matter of the unseen (ghayb)? Will you repent and return to the clear truths that all rational people affirm?

intermediate
6 min
The Sophist Argument for Suspension of Judgment
The sophist argument and the author's self-contradiction response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the sophist argument that leads to suspension of judgment and the author's counter-argument showing its self-contradiction.

Core Idea
Extreme skepticism collapses under its own argument.

The author now addresses the fourth and most radical sophist sect, which argues that all sources of knowledge—sensory, imaginative, and rational—are suspect. They conclude that we must suspend all judgment. In this lesson, we will see the sophist's reasoning and the author's initial refutation that the sophist's own statement undermines itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the sophist's argument
Identify the three judges they mention and their reasoning for rejecting each.
2
Understand the conclusion of tawaqquf
See why they argue that only suspension of judgment remains.
3
Examine the author's response
The author points out that the sophist's own claim leads to self-contradiction or infinite doubt.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفرقة الرابعة السوفسطائية الذين قدموا في الحسيات والبديهيات. وقالوا ظهر بكلام الفريقين تطرق التهمة إلى الحاكم الحسى والخيالي والعقلى ، فلا بد وأن يكون في حاكم آخر فوقها ، ولا يجوز أن يكون ذلك الحاكم هو الاستدلال لأنه فرعها ، ولو معناهما به لزم الدور ولا نجد حاكما آخر فوقها ، وإذا لا طريق إلا التوقف . لا يقال : هذا الكلام الذى ذكر تم إن أفا. ك علما بفساد الحسبات والبديهيات قد ناقضت وإلا قد اعترفت بسقوطه . لانا نقول : هذا الكلام الذي ذكرته أنت يفيد القطع بالثبوت والذى ذكرته أنا يفيد التهمة والشك إنما يتولد من هذه المآخذ ، فأناشاك وشاك في أنى شاك وهلم جرا . واعلم أن الاشتغال بالجواب عن هذه الشبهة يحصل غرضهم على ما قرروه في كلماتهم ، فالصواب أنا لا نشتغل بالجواب عنها ؛ لأنا تعلم أن علمنا بأن الواحد نصف الاثنين وأن النار حارة والشمس مضيئة لا يزول بما ذكروه. بل الطريق أن يعذبوا حتى يعترفوا بالحسبات ، وإذا اعترفوا بالحسيات فقد اعترفوا بالبديهيات ، أعنى الفرق بين وجود الألم وعدمه ، وأما الأجوبة المفصلة عن هذه الأسئلة فسيجيء في الأبواب المستقيلة إن شاء الله تعالى
The fourth sect: the sophists, who advanced [arguments] concerning sensory and self-evident truths. They said: It has become clear from the statements of the two groups that suspicion reaches the sensory, imaginative, and rational judge, so there must be another judge above them, and it is not permissible for that judge to be inference because it is a branch of them, and if we mean both by it, circularity would occur, and we find no other judge above them, so there is no way except suspension. It should not be said: 'This statement that you have mentioned, if it gives knowledge of the corruption of sensory and self-evident truths, then you have contradicted yourself, otherwise you have admitted its fall.' But we say: This statement that you have mentioned yields certainty of affirmation, while what I have mentioned yields suspicion, and doubt is only generated from these premises, so you are doubtful and I am doubtful whether I am doubtful, and so on. Know that engaging in answering this doubt achieves their purpose as they have stated in their words. The correct course is that we do not engage in answering it, because we know that our knowledge that one is half of two and that fire is hot and the sun is bright does not vanish due to what they have mentioned. Rather, the way is to torture them until they admit sensory truths, and if they admit sensory truths, they have admitted self-evident truths, namely the difference between the existence of pain and its absence. As for detailed answers to these questions, they will come in the coming chapters, God willing.
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage discusses the fourth sophist sect, which argues that because both sensory and rational judgments can be suspected, there must be a higher judge, but inference is dependent on them and circular, so only suspension of judgment remains. The author then presents a response: the sophist's own statement either yields knowledge (contradicting their skepticism) or is not knowledge. The author then gives his own answer: the sophist's claim only produces suspicion, not certainty, while our knowledge of basic truths (like one is half of two) remains firm. Therefore, we should not engage in detailed refutation; instead, physical coercion may be used to make them admit sensory truths, which leads to admitting self-evident truths. Detailed refutations will be given later.

Key Terms
السوفسطائية
التوقف
الحسيات
البديهيات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the sophist's argument is logically unassailable.
Correction: The author shows that the sophist's argument is self-defeating because it claims certainty about the unreliability of all knowledge.

Checkpoint
What is the sophist's conclusion from their argument?
A) Sensory truths are reliable.
B) Inference is the highest judge.
C) We must suspend all judgment.
D) The imaginative judge is the highest.

Key Takeaway
Extreme skepticism leads to self-contradiction because the claim that all knowledge is suspect must itself be knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever used doubt as an excuse to avoid accepting a clear truth about your own faults? In what area of your life today are you hiding behind 'I'm not sure' when you really know? What would this lesson's exposure of self-defeating skepticism say to your heart right now?
?
The sophist claimed no judgment is safe from suspicion—yet his own claim assumed certainty. Where in your life are you making an absolute claim while pretending to be open-minded? Will you let this lesson unmask that inconsistency and compel you to take a stand?
?
If every argument you make against truth is itself a claim to know something, then your skepticism is dishonest. Is there a truth you have been resisting because accepting it would demand a change you are unwilling to make? When will you stop the infinite regress and submit?

The Author's Method: Coercion for Stubborn Skeptics
The author advocates coercion as a response to radical skepticism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's practical method for dealing with obstinate sophists and the role of coercion in establishing foundational truths.

Core Idea
Foundational truths are so evident that denial warrants coercion, not debate.

Having refuted the sophist's argument on logical grounds, the author now presents his own approach: refuse to engage in debate with those who deny sensory truths. Instead, he advocates using physical coercion to force admission of these undeniable truths. This lesson explores the reasoning behind this method and its limits.

Guided Path
1
Read the author's warning
Engaging in debate gives the sophist what they want; it is counterproductive.
2
Understand the unshakable nature of basic truths
Our knowledge of truths like 'one is half of two' is not affected by sophist arguments.
3
Learn the coercive method
The author suggests torture until they admit sensory truths, which then leads to admission of self-evident truths.
4
Note the deferral of detailed refutations
Detailed answers are promised for later chapters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفرقة الرابعة السوفسطائية الذين قدموا في الحسيات والبديهيات. وقالوا ظهر بكلام الفريقين تطرق التهمة إلى الحاكم الحسى والخيالي والعقلى ، فلا بد وأن يكون في حاكم آخر فوقها ، ولا يجوز أن يكون ذلك الحاكم هو الاستدلال لأنه فرعها ، ولو معناهما به لزم الدور ولا نجد حاكما آخر فوقها ، وإذا لا طريق إلا التوقف . لا يقال : هذا الكلام الذى ذكر تم إن أفا. ك علما بفساد الحسبات والبديهيات قد ناقضت وإلا قد اعترفت بسقوطه . لانا نقول : هذا الكلام الذي ذكرته أنت يفيد القطع بالثبوت والذى ذكرته أنا يفيد التهمة والشك إنما يتولد من هذه المآخذ ، فأناشاك وشاك في أنى شاك وهلم جرا . واعلم أن الاشتغال بالجواب عن هذه الشبهة يحصل غرضهم على ما قرروه في كلماتهم ، فالصواب أنا لا نشتغل بالجواب عنها ؛ لأنا تعلم أن علمنا بأن الواحد نصف الاثنين وأن النار حارة والشمس مضيئة لا يزول بما ذكروه. بل الطريق أن يعذبوا حتى يعترفوا بالحسبات ، وإذا اعترفوا بالحسيات فقد اعترفوا بالبديهيات ، أعنى الفرق بين وجود الألم وعدمه ، وأما الأجوبة المفصلة عن هذه الأسئلة فسيجيء في الأبواب المستقيلة إن شاء الله تعالى
The fourth sect: the sophists, who advanced [arguments] concerning sensory and self-evident truths. They said: It has become clear from the statements of the two groups that suspicion reaches the sensory, imaginative, and rational judge, so there must be another judge above them, and it is not permissible for that judge to be inference because it is a branch of them, and if we mean both by it, circularity would occur, and we find no other judge above them, so there is no way except suspension. It should not be said: 'This statement that you have mentioned, if it gives knowledge of the corruption of sensory and self-evident truths, then you have contradicted yourself, otherwise you have admitted its fall.' But we say: This statement that you have mentioned yields certainty of affirmation, while what I have mentioned yields suspicion, and doubt is only generated from these premises, so you are doubtful and I am doubtful whether I am doubtful, and so on. Know that engaging in answering this doubt achieves their purpose as they have stated in their words. The correct course is that we do not engage in answering it, because we know that our knowledge that one is half of two and that fire is hot and the sun is bright does not vanish due to what they have mentioned. Rather, the way is to torture them until they admit sensory truths, and if they admit sensory truths, they have admitted self-evident truths, namely the difference between the existence of pain and its absence. As for detailed answers to these questions, they will come in the coming chapters, God willing.
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage discusses the fourth sophist sect, which argues that because both sensory and rational judgments can be suspected, there must be a higher judge, but inference is dependent on them and circular, so only suspension of judgment remains. The author then presents a response: the sophist's own statement either yields knowledge (contradicting their skepticism) or is not knowledge. Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the author advocates torture as a universal method for all skeptics.
Correction: The author applies this only to extreme sophists who deny obvious sensory truths, and he promises detailed refutations later.

Checkpoint
What does the author say about our knowledge of basic truths like 'one is half of two'?
A) It can be doubted.
B) It is unshakable and not affected by sophist arguments.
C) It depends on inference.
D) It is only known after torture.

Key Takeaway
For foundational sensory truths, debate is futile; practical coercion may be used to force admission, but detailed logical refutation is postponed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter someone who denies something you hold as absolutely certain—like the reality of your own pain—do you feel the urge to debate or to take action? What does this passage reveal about the limits of your own patience with skepticism?
?
The author suggests coercion for stubborn deniers of sensory truths. Have you ever been so certain about a truth that you felt justified in using force, even if only verbally, to make someone admit it? How does this teaching challenge you to examine whether your certainties are truly foundational?
?
Are there 'sensory truths' in your spiritual life—experiences of God's existence, mercy, or discipline—that you have allowed doubts to cloud? What would it take for you to treat those doubts as the author treats sophist doubts, refusing to entertain them and reaffirming what you know without debate?

The Sects of Sophists and the Greek Origin of the Term
Description of sophist sects and etymology of the term.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three sects of Sophists and the linguistic origin of the term 'sophistry'.

Core Idea
Sophism is not a school but a label for error, derived from Greek words for wisdom and mistake.

This lesson explores the commentator's clarification of the Sophist phenomenon. He identifies three subgroups and traces the word back to Greek, revealing that sophistry is not a coherent philosophy but a term for systematic error.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the three sects are presented in a compact manner.
2
Identify the three sects
Find the three sects mentioned: La-adriyya, 'Inadiyya, and 'Indiyya. Understand the core stance of each.
Each sect's name reflects its position: doubt, obstinacy, relativism.
3
Examine the etymology
The commentator explains the Greek origin of 'safsata' and 'falsafa'. Note the components: 'sufa' (wisdom) and 'ista' (error).
This linguistic analysis helps demystify the term.
4
Reflect on the misconception
Consider why the commentator emphasizes that no real group adopts this doctrine. This will be built upon in the next lesson.
The key insight: sophistry is an action, not a school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقول : إن قوما من الناس يظنون أن السوفسطائية قوم لهم نحلة ويتشعبون إلى ثلاث طوائف اللا أدرية وهم الذين قالوا نحن شاكون وشاكون فى أنا شاكون وهلم جرا ، والعنادية وهم الذين يقولون ما من قضية بديهية أو نظرية إلا ولها معارضة ومقاومة بثليها فى القوة والقبول عند الأذهان ، والعندية وهم الذين يقولون مذهب كل قوم حق بالقياس إليهم وباطل بالقياس إلى خصومهم ، وقد يكون طرفا النقيض حقا بالقياس إلى شخصين وليس فى نفس الأمر شيء حق ، وأما أهل التحقيق فقد قالوا : هذه لفظة من لغة اليونانيين فإن ( سوفا ) بلغتهم اسم العلم أو الحكمة ) واسطا ) اسم للغلط فسوفسطا كمعناه علم الغلط كما كان (فيلا) اسم للحب ، وفيلسوف معناه محب العلم ثم عرب هذان اللفظان واشتق منهما السفسطة والفلسفة قالوا : وليس ولا يمكن أن يكون فى العالم قوم ينتحلون هذا المذهب بل كل غالط سوفسطائى فى موضع غلطه ، وكثير من الناس متحيرون لا مذهب لهم أصلا . وقد رتب مثل هذه الأسئلة والإيرادات ذلك المنحيرون من طلبة العلم وأسندوها إلى السوفسطائيين والله أعلم بحقيقة الحال . والطريق الذى ذكره صاحب الكتاب أمنى التعذيب إنما اختاروه لأخذ الاعتراف منهم ببعض القضايا الواجب قبولها ليتمكنوا من الإرشادهم أو البحث معهم بناء على ما اعترفوا به. فهذا ما عندى في هذه المباحث ، والحق أن تصدير كتاب الأصول الدينية بمثل هذا الكلام يقتضى تضليل طلاب الحق . والله ولي التوفيق .
I say: Some people think that the Sophists are a group with a school, and they branch into three sects: the La-adriyya (agnostics), who say 'We are doubtful and doubtful that we are doubtful, and so on'; the 'Inadiyya (obstinate), who say that there is no self-evident or theoretical proposition except that it has an opposite and resistance equal in strength and acceptance to minds; and the 'Indiyya (relativists), who say that every group's doctrine is true relative to them and false relative to their opponents, and the two extremes of a contradiction may both be true relative to two persons, and there is nothing true in itself. The investigators have said: This is a word from the Greek language: 'Sufa' in their language means knowledge or wisdom, and 'ista' means error, so 'sufasta' means the science of error, just as 'fila' means love, and 'faylasuf' means lover of wisdom. Then these two words were Arabized and from them were derived 'safsata' and 'falsafa'. They said: There is not, and cannot be in the world, a group that adopts this doctrine; rather, everyone who is mistaken is a sophist in the place of his error. Many people are confused and have no doctrine at all. Such questions and objections have been arranged by these confused students of knowledge and attributed to the Sophists; God knows the true state of affairs. The method mentioned by the author, namely torture, they chose only to obtain from them admission of some necessarily acceptable propositions so that they could guide them or debate with them based on what they admitted. This is what I have in these investigations. The truth is that introducing such speech in a book on the fundamentals of religion necessitates misleading seekers of truth. God is the grantor of success.
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage is a commentator's note that clarifies the nature of Sophism. It states that some people mistakenly think Sophists are a unified school, but actually they are divided into three sects: the agnostics (la-adriyya) who doubt everything including their doubt, the obstinate ('inadiyya) who claim every proposition has an equal counter, and the relativists ('indiyya) who hold that truth is relative. The commentator reports that investigators derive the term from Greek: 'sufa' (wisdom) and 'ista' (error), meaning 'science of error'. They assert that no real group adopts this doctrine; anyone mistaken is a sophist in that instance. The commentator also criticizes the author (Imam Razi) for including such material in a theology book, saying it misleads seekers of truth. The method of torture is justified as a means to obtain basic admissions for further guidance or debate.

Key Terms
السوفسطائية
اللا أدرية
العنادية
العندية
السفسطة
الفلسفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Sophists are a coherent philosophical school with a unified doctrine.
Correction: The investigator states that there is no actual group that adopts sophism; it is merely a label for erroneous reasoning.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the core belief of the 'Inadiyya sect?
A) They doubt everything including their doubt.
B) They believe every proposition has an equal counterargument.
C) They think truth is relative to each person.
D) They deny the existence of knowledge.

Key Takeaway
The Sophists are not a unified school but three distinct sects, and the term 'sophistry' originates from Greek words meaning 'science of error'.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever met someone who doubts everything so much that they even doubt their own reasoning? How did that affect your trust in knowledge? Are you ever guilty of that yourself?
?
When you find yourself thinking 'that might be true for you but not for me,' are you falling into the relativism of the 'Indiyya? What would it mean to actually commit to objective truth?
?
If sophistry means 'science of error,' how careful are you about your own sources of knowledge? Do you ever uncritically accept ideas just because they sound wise? Will you start examining the roots of what you believe?

The Reality of Sophistry and Its Misplacement in Theology
Critique of including sophistry in theology and the method of torture.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the commentator criticizes the inclusion of sophist discussions in theology books and the controversial method of torture.

Core Idea
No true school of sophistry exists; including it in theology misleads students.

In this lesson, the commentator argues that sophistry is not an actual doctrine but a label for anyone who is mistaken. He also criticizes the use of torture to gain admissions and questions the wisdom of discussing such topics in a book on religious fundamentals.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the shift from classification to criticism.
2
Understand the investigator's claim
The investigators assert that no real group adopts sophism; it's only applied to individuals in error. Many are simply confused.
This reinforces that sophistry is not a school.
3
Evaluate the torture method
The commentator mentions that the author (Imam Razi) suggested torture to extract admissions for guidance. The commentator implies this is problematic.
Consider whether this method is ethical in your own view.
4
Reflect on the criticism
The commentator concludes that discussing sophistry in a theology book misleads seekers of truth. Think about why he says this.
This is a methodological critique of Imam Razi's work.

?

?
This passage says that including such debates at the start of theology books misleads seekers. Where in your own spiritual life do you waste time on side arguments or doubts that are not real, instead of focusing on foundational beliefs? Will you commit today to prioritize core truths?
?
The method of torture is used to force admissions. In your own interactions, do you ever pressure others (or yourself) into accepting premises through harsh reasoning rather than building on shared ground gently? How can you change your approach to be a guide, not an interrogator?

The Three Sects of Sophists and Their Origin
Editor classifies sophists into three sects and gives linguistic derivation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the editor's classification of sophists into three sects and the Greek linguistic derivation of the term 'sophist'.

Core Idea
The sophists are not a real school but a label for individual error.

In this editor's note, we encounter a classification of the so-called sophists into three distinct sects: the Agnostics, the Obstinate, and the Relativists. The editor also traces the word 'sophist' to its Greek roots, revealing its original meaning as 'science of error'. This passage sets the stage for understanding how Muslim theologians treated skeptical arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully.
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get the editor's classification of sophists.
2
Identify the three sects.
Find the names and definitions of the three sects: La-adriyya, 'Inadiyya, and 'Indiyya.
Notice how each sect's position is described.
3
Understand the linguistic derivation.
The editor explains that 'sophist' comes from Greek words meaning 'knowledge' and 'error'. Reflect on why this etymology is significant.
The term 'sophist' originally means 'science of error', not 'lover of wisdom'.
4
Consider the editor's overall claim.
The editor argues that no real school of sophists ever existed; instead, error is individual. How does this frame the classification?

أقول : إن قوما من الناس يظنون أن السوفسطائية قوم لهم نحلة ويتشعبون إلى ثلاث طوائف اللا أدرية وهم الذين قالوا نحن شاكون وشاكون فى أنا شاكون وهلم جرا ، والعنادية وهم الذين يقولون ما من قضية بديهية أو نظرية إلا ولها معارضة ومقاومة بثليها فى القوة والقبول عند الأذهان ، والعندية وهم الذين يقولون مذهب كل قوم حق بالقياس إليهم وباطل بالقياس إلى خصومهم ، وقد يكون طرفا النقيض حقا بالقياس إلى شخصين وليس فى نفس الأمر شيء حق ، وأما أهل التحقيق فقد قالوا : هذه لفظة من لغة اليونانيين فإن ( سوفا ) بلغتهم اسم العلم أو الحكمة ) واسطا ) اسم للغلط فسوفسطا كمعناه علم الغلط كما كان (فيلا) اسم للحب ، وفيلسوف معناه محب العلم ثم عرب هذان اللفظان واشتق منهما السفسطة والفلسفة قالوا : وليس ولا يمكن أن يكون فى العالم قوم ينتحلون هذا المذهب بل كل غالط سوفسطائى فى موضع غلطه ، وكثير من الناس متحيرون لا مذهب لهم أصلا . وقد رتب مثل هذه الأسئلة والإيرادات ذلك المنحيرون من طلبة العلم وأسندوها إلى السوفسطائيين والله أعلم بحقيقة الحال . والتاريق الذى ذكره صاحب الكتاب أمنى التعذيب إنما اختاروه لأخذ الاعتراف منهم ببعض القضايا الواجب قبولها ليتمكنوا من الإرشادهم أو البحث معهم بناء على ما اعترفوا به. فهذا ما عندى في هذه المباحث ، والحق أن تصدير كتاب الأصول الدينية بمثل هذا الكلام يقتضى تضليل طلاب الحق . والله ولي التوفيق .
I say: Some people think that the Sophists are a group with a school, branching into three sects: the La-adriyya (Agnostics), who say 'We are doubting and doubting that we are doubting' and so on; the 'Inadiyya (Obstinate), who say no proposition, intuitive or theoretical, but has an opposite equal in strength and acceptance by minds; the 'Indiyya (Relativists), who say every group's doctrine is true relative to them and false relative to their opponents, and two contradictory things may both be true relative to two persons, but in reality nothing is true. The verifiers said: This is a word from the Greek language: 'Sofa' in their language means knowledge or wisdom, and 'ista' means error, so 'sofista' means the science of error, just as 'fila' means love, and 'faylasuf' means lover of knowledge. Then these two words were Arabized and derived from them 'safsaṭa' and 'falsafa'. They said: There is not, nor can there be, a people who adopt this school; rather, every person who makes an error is a sophist in the place of his error. Many people are confused and have no school at all. Such questions and objections were arranged by those confused students of knowledge and attributed to the Sophists. God knows the truth of the matter. The path that the author mentioned — the punishment — they only chose it to obtain from them confession to some necessary propositions so that they can guide them or debate them based on what they confessed. This is my view on these discussions. The truth is that opening a book on religious foundations with such speech leads to misleading seekers of truth. God is the granter of success.
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This is an editor's note. It first classifies the Sophists into three sects: Agnostics (who doubt everything), Obstinate (who claim every proposition has an equally strong opposite), and Relativists (who make truth relative). Then it provides a linguistic derivation: the term 'sophist' comes from Greek words meaning 'wisdom' and 'error,' hence 'science of error.' The editor argues that there never was an actual school of Sophists; rather, errors are individual. The note then comments on the author's method of punishment: it is a pedagogical tool to secure minimal agreement for debate. The editor criticizes the author for including such material in a book on religious principles, as it may mislead seekers of truth.

Key Terms
سوفسطائية
لا أدرية
عنادية
عندية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many people think the sophists were a real, coherent school of thought with three distinct sects.
Correction: The editor argues that no such school ever existed; the term refers to individual error, and the classification is based on common patterns of error.

Checkpoint
What is the original meaning of 'sophist' according to the editor?
A) Lover of wisdom
B) Science of error
C) Denier of truth
D) Greek philosopher

Key Takeaway
The editor classifies sophists into three sects but argues that they do not form a real school; rather, the term refers to individual error.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever been like the Obstinate sect, holding to an opinion even when faced with an equally strong opposite? What is one belief you currently cling to that you have not critically examined? Commit today to seeking the truth about it, even if it means admitting you were wrong.
?
The Relativists say that truth is relative to each person. In what area of your life do you excuse your own actions by saying 'it's true for me' or 'it's my truth'? How does this contradict the Islamic belief in an absolute truth revealed by Allah? What step will you take to align your life with that absolute truth?
?
The editor says that every person who makes an error is a sophist in that instance. Think of a recent mistake you made. Did you try to justify it by blaming a 'school of thought' or external factors? Will you instead own it as your personal error and actively correct it, seeking knowledge to avoid repeating it?

The Editor's Critique of Including Sophist Arguments
Editor criticizes the author's method and inclusion of sophist arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the editor's criticism of using sophist arguments in religious texts and his explanation of the pedagogical purpose of the punishment method.

Core Idea
Including sophist arguments in religious books can mislead seekers of truth.

In the final part of his note, the editor addresses the author's method of using punishment to secure agreement from sophists. He argues that this is a pedagogical tool to establish common ground for debate. More importantly, he criticizes the very inclusion of such material in a book on religious principles, warning that it may lead students astray.

Guided Path
1
Read the final part of the passage.
Read from 'والتاريق الذى ذكره' to the end, focusing on the editor's comments.
2
Understand the punishment method.
The editor explains that the author used punishment to get sophists to admit to basic truths. Reflect on why this might be effective.
Punishment is not for coercion but to secure a starting point for debate.
3
Consider the editor's criticism.
The editor warns that opening a book on religious foundations with such discussions misleads students. Why might he think so?

This is an editor's note. It first classifies the Sophists into three sects: Agnostics (who doubt everything), Obstinate (who claim every proposition has an equally strong opposite), and Relativists (who make truth relative). Then it provides a linguistic derivation: the term 'sophist' comes from Greek words meaning 'wisdom' and 'error,' hence 'science of error.' The editor argues that there never was an actual school of Sophists; rather, errors are individual. The note then comments on the author's method of punishment: it is a pedagogical tool to secure minimal agreement for debate. The editor criticizes the author for including such material in a book on religious principles, as it may mislead seekers of truth.

Key Terms
سوفسطائية
لا أدرية
عنادية
عندية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the punishment was used to force belief or conversion.
Correction: The editor clarifies it was only to obtain confession to necessary propositions for the sake of debate.

Checkpoint
Why did the author use the punishment method according to the editor?
A) To force sophists to adopt Islam
B) To obtain confession to some necessary propositions
C) To demonstrate the power of the ruler
D) To mock the sophists

Key Takeaway
The editor criticizes the inclusion of sophist arguments in foundational religious texts, as it can lead seekers of truth astray.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The editor says that opening a book on religious foundations with sophist arguments misleads seekers of truth. Has any book or teacher you've relied on ever caused you confusion by presenting doubts before establishing certainties? What did you do to resolve that?
?
If you were to write a book on 'aqeedah, would you include the arguments of the sophists? Why or why not? Be honest: Are you more concerned with appearing scholarly or with protecting your reader?
?
The author's method was to first obtain confessions to necessary propositions. Reflect: In your own debates about religion, do you first establish common ground with your interlocutor, or do you jump into refutation? How can you apply the editor's caution to your own teaching or learning?

Mu'tazilite View on Non-Existent Essences
Summary of Mu'tazilite agreement on non-existent possible things having essences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite position that possible non-existent things have real essences before existence, and that God's creative act only bestows existence, not essence.

Core Idea
Possible things have essences before they exist; creation only makes them existent.

This lesson introduces a key Mu'tazilite doctrine: non-existent possible things already possess fixed essences. According to prominent Mu'tazilite thinkers, God's role is not to create these essences but to bring them into existence. This view has significant implications for understanding divine power and the nature of possibility.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scholars
Read the list of Mu'tazilite scholars mentioned. They represent a consensus within the school on this issue.
Names include al-Shahham, al-Jubba'i, Abu Hashim, etc.
2
Understand the claim about essences
Note that they claim non-existent possible things have essences (dhawat), entities (a'yan), and realities (haqa'iq) before existence.
This is a metaphysical claim about the status of possibles.
3
Distinguish agent's effect
The agent does not make them essences; He makes those essences existent. This is a crucial distinction between essence and existence.
Essences are uncreated and fixed.
4
Recognize the agreements
They agree that essences are distinct by their individuals, and that each type has an infinite number of fixed essences.
This implies a form of realism about universals and individuals.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
زعم أبو يعقوب الشحام وأبو على الجبائى وابنه أبو هاشم وأبو الحمد بن الخياط وأبو عبد الله البصرى وأبو إسحاق بن عياش والقاضى عبد الجبار بن أحمد وتلامذته أن المعدومات الممكنة قبل دخولها في الوجود ذوات وأعيان وحقائق وأن تأثير الفاعل ليس فى جعلها ذوات بل فى جعل تلك الذوات موجودة ، واتفقوا على أن تلك الذوات متباينة بأشخاصها ، واتفقوا على أن الثابت من كل نوع من تلك المعدومات عدد غير متناه .
Abu Ya'qub al-Shahham, Abu 'Ali al-Jubba'i and his son Abu Hashim, Abu al-Hamd ibn al-Khayyat, Abu 'Abd Allah al-Basri, Abu Ishaq ibn 'Ayyash, al-Qadi 'Abd al-Jabbar ibn Ahmad and their students claimed that possible non-existent things before entering into existence are essences, entities, and realities, and that the effect of the agent is not in making them essences but in making those essences existent. And they agreed that those essences are distinct by their individuals, and they agreed that the fixed from each type of those non-existent things is an infinite number.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage summarizes the agreed position of a group of prominent Mu'tazilites (including al-Shahham, al-Jubba'i, Abu Hashim, etc.) on the nature of non-existent possible things. They hold that such things, before coming into existence, have real essences (dhawat), entities (a'yan), and realities (haqa'iq). The agent (al-fa'il, i.e., God) does not create these essences; rather, He makes them existent. These essences are distinct from each other in their individuality, and the number of fixed essences of each type is infinite.

Correction: They actually believe essences are uncreated and fixed; only existence is granted by God.

B) Makes the essences existent without creating their essences.
C) Destroys the essences.
D) None of the above.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilites held that possible things have eternal, fixed essences, and God's creative act only brings them into existence, not creates their essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your essence—your core self—was already fixed before you were born, does that make you feel more or less accountable for who you become? What will you do today to align your actions with that fixed essence?
?
The Mu'tazilites say God's act only gives existence, not essence. How does that affect your prayer life—do you pray only for existence needs, or also for transformation of your essence? Name one concrete change you will seek in your character this week.

Mu'tazilite Common Ground: Essences Equal, Attributes Different
Mu'tazilite agreement on essences and attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the foundational agreement among Mu'tazilites who affirm non-existent essences: all essences are equal in being essences, and differences arise solely through attributes.

Core Idea
All Mu'tazilites agree: essences are equal, attributes make the difference.

Before exploring the detailed disagreements within the Mu'tazilite school, it is crucial to grasp the common starting point. All Mu'tazilites who accept non-existent essences share a foundational belief: these essences are identical in their essence-hood, and any distinction between them comes solely from attributes. This lesson examines that shared ground.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the sense. Then read the translation. Notice the key technical terms: 'ذوات' (essences), 'صفات' (attributes).
2
Identify the common agreement
The passage states two points of agreement: (1) All non-existent essences are equal in being essences. (2) Differences among them are only through attributes. Paraphrase this in your own words.
This is the starting point for the entire discussion.
3
Consider the significance
Why is it important that they agree on the equality of essences? Think about the implications for the ontological status of attributes. Attributes are what provide differentiation.
4
Preview the upcoming disagreements
The passage then says 'ثم اختلفوا' (then they disagreed). This signals that the common ground is only the starting point. The next lessons will explore the nature of the disagreement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما المعتزلة فقد اتفق القائلون منهم بالذوات المعدومة على أنها بأسرها متساوية في كونها ذوات ، وأن الاختلاف بينها ليس إلا بالصفات، ثم اختلفوا فذهب الجمهور منهم إلى أنها موصوفة بصفات الأجناس ومرادهم منها أن ذوات الجواهر موصوفة بصفة الجوهرية وذات السواد موصوفة بصفة السوادية وهلم جراً . وزعم ابن عياش أن تلك الذوات عارية عن جميع الصفات والصفات لا تحصل إلا زمان الوجود . القائلون بالصفات زعموا أن صفات الجواهر إما أن تكون عائدة إلى الجملة وهى الحياة وكل ما كان مشروطا بها أو إلى الأفراد وهى إما فى الجواهر أو فى الأعراض ، أما الجواهر فقد أثبتوا لها صفات أربعة أحدها الصفة الحاصلة حالتى العدم والوجود وهى الجوهرية والثانية الوجود وهو الصفة الحاصلة بالفاعل . الثالثة التحيز وهو الصفة التابعة للحدوث والصادرة عن صفة الجوهرية بشرط الوجود . والرابعة الحصول في الحيز وهو الصفة المعللة بالمعنى . قالوا : وليس للجوهر الفرد صفة زائدة على هذه الأربع فليس له بكونه أسود وأبيض صفة وكذا القول فى كل عرض غير مشروط بالحياة . وأما الأعراض فالصفات العائدة إلى الجملة غير معقولة فيها وأما العائدة إلى الآحاد فعلته الصفة الحاصلة حالتى العدم والوجود والصفة الصادرة عنها عند الوجود صفة الوجود . فهذا هو المذهب الذي استقر جمهورهم عليه وهو قول أبي على وأبى هاشم والقاضى عبد الجبار وأنى رشيد وابن مثوبة . ومنهم من خالف هذا التفصيل في مواضع أحدها : أن أبا يعقوب والشحام وأبا عبد الله البصرى وأبا إسحاق بن عياش زعموا أن الجوهرية هي التحيز ثم اختلفوا بعد ذلك فزعم الشحام وأبو عبد الله أن
As for the Mu'tazilites, those among them who affirm non-existent essences agreed that all of them are equal in being essences, and that difference among them is only through attributes. Then they disagreed. The majority went to the view that they are qualified by genus-attributes, meaning that the essence of substances is qualified by the attribute of substantiality, the essence of blackness by the attribute of blackness, and so on. Ibn 'Ayyash claimed that those essences are bare of all attributes, and attributes are only attained at the time of existence. Those who affirm attributes claimed that the attributes of substances are either returning to the whole (al-jumla), which is life and all that is conditional upon it, or to individuals, which are either in substances or in accidents. As for substances, they affirmed four attributes for them: first, the attribute obtaining in both states of non-existence and existence, which is substantiality; second, existence, which is the attribute obtained from the agent; third, occupying space (tahayyuz), which is the attribute following upon origination and emanating from the attribute of substantiality under the condition of existence; fourth, being located in a place (al-husul fi al-hayyiz), which is the attribute caused by a meaning. They said: The individual substance has no attribute beyond these four, so it has no attribute of being black or white, and similarly for every accident not conditional upon life. As for accidents, attributes returning to the whole are not intelligible in them, and as for attributes returning to individuals, they have the attribute obtaining in both states of non-existence and existence, and the attribute emanating from it at existence is the attribute of existence. This is the doctrine upon which their majority settled, and it is the saying of Abu 'Ali, Abu Hashim, al-Qadi 'Abd al-Jabbar, Abu Rashid, and Ibn Mathwa. Some of them opposed this detail in certain respects. One of them: Abu Ya'qub, al-Shahham, Abu 'Abd Allah al-Basri, and Abu Ishaq ibn 'Ayyash claimed that substantiality is occupying space. Then they disagreed after that. Al-Shahham and Abu 'Abd Allah claimed that...
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage details the Mu'tazilite positions on whether non-existent essences have attributes. All Mu'tazilites who affirm non-existent essences agree that all essences are equal in being essences, and differences arise only through attributes. They then divided: the majority (jumhur) held that essences are qualified by genus-attributes (sifat al-ajnas), such as substantiality for substances and blackness for blackness. Ibn 'Ayyash held that essences are bare of all attributes until existence. The majority then provided a detailed classification of attributes for substances: four attributes: substantiality (obtains in both non-existence and existence), existence (from the agent), occupying space (follows origination), and being in a place (caused by a meaning). They denied other attributes like color. For accidents, only two attributes: the attribute obtaining in both states and the attribute of existence. This is the settled doctrine of the majority. Some dissented: al-Shahham and others identified substantiality with occupying space, but the text cuts off before completing their view.

Key Terms
الصفات
ذوات

Key Terms from Source Passage

According to the passage, what do all Mu'tazilites who affirm non-existent essences agree upon?
A) Non-existent essences are not equal.
B) Essences are equal, and differences come from attributes.
C) Attributes are the same as essences.
D) There is no agreement among them.

Key Takeaway
All Mu'tazilites who accept non-existent essences agree that essences are identical in themselves and differ only through attributes.

First Disagreement: Do Non-Existent Essences Have Genus-Attributes?
First Mu'tazilite disagreement on attributes of non-existent essences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first major split among Mu'tazilites: the majority held that non-existent essences are qualified by genus-attributes, while Ibn 'Ayyash argued they are bare of all attributes until existence.

Core Idea
Do essences have attributes before existence? Majority says yes, Ibn 'Ayyash says no.

Having established the common ground, we now encounter a sharp divide. The majority (jumhur) of Mu'tazilites maintained that each non-existent essence possesses a genus-attribute (sifat al-jins), such as substantiality for substances or blackness for blackness. Ibn 'Ayyash, on the other hand, claimed that essences are completely devoid of attributes before they come into existence. This lesson contrasts these two positions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the two contrasting views introduced by 'ذهب الجمهور' and 'وزعم ابن عياش'.
2
Identify the majority view
The majority say essences are described by genus-attributes. Understand what 'صفات الأجناس' means: for each kind of essence, there is a defining attribute (e.g., جوهرية for substances).
3
Identify Ibn 'Ayyash's view
He holds that essences are bare of all attributes, and attributes only come into being at the time of existence ('عند الوجود').
4
Compare the two positions
Ask yourself: what is at stake? Does the majority view imply that attributes are co-eternal with essences? How does Ibn 'Ayyash's view preserve the radical distinction between non-existence and existence?

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?

Majority Classification: Four Attributes for Substances, Two for Accidents
Detailed majority classification of attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the detailed classification of attributes that the majority of Mu'tazilites assigned to substances and accidents, including the four attributes for substances and the two for accidents.

Core Idea
Substances have four attributes: substantiality, existence, occupancy, and location.

The majority of Mu'tazilites not only held that non-existent essences have genus-attributes, but they further developed a sophisticated classification of attributes for substances and accidents. For substances, they identified four attributes: substantiality (present in both non-existence and existence), existence (from the agent), occupying space (following origination), and being in a place (caused by a meaning). For accidents, they limited attributes to two. This lesson unpacks this classification and lists the prominent scholars who held this view.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice the division: attributes of substances versus attributes of accidents, and within substances, the four specific attributes.
2
Identify the four attributes for substances
List them: 1) substantiality (al-jawhariyya), 2) existence (al-wujud), 3) occupying space (al-tahayyuz), 4) being in a place (al-husul fi al-hayyiz). Understand the explanation for each.
The first is present in both non-existence and existence; the second comes from the agent; the third follows origination; the fourth is caused by a meaning.
3
Understand attributes for accidents
Accidents only have two attributes: the attribute that obtains in both states (like substantiality for substances) and the attribute of existence (from the agent).
Attributes returning to the whole (like life) are not applicable to accidents.
4
Note the list of scholars
The passage names Abu 'Ali, Abu Hashim, al-Qadi 'Abd al-Jabbar, Abu Rashid, and Ibn Mathwa as holding this view. Memorizing them can help contextualize the school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما المعتزلة فقد اتفق القائلون منهم بالذوات المعدومة على أنها بأسرها متساوية في كونها ذوات ، وأن الاختلاف بينها ليس إلا بالصفات، ثم اختلفوا فذهب الجمهور منهم إلى أنها موصوفة بصفات الأجناس ومرادهم منها أن ذوات الجواهر موصوفة بصفة الجوهرية وذات السواد موصوفة بصفة السوادية وهلم جراً . وزعم ابن عياش أن تلك الذوات عارية عن جميع الصفات والصفات لا تحصل إلا زمان الوجود . القائلون بالصفات زعموا أن صفات الجواهر إما أن تكون عائدة إلى الجملة وهى الحياة وكل ما كان مشروطا بها أو إلى الأفراد وهى إما فى الجواهر أو فى الأعراض ، أما الجواهر فقد أثبتوا لها صفات أربعة أحدها الصفة الحاصلة حالتى العدم والوجود وهى الجوهرية والثانية الوجود وهو الصفة الحاصلة بالفاعل . الثالثة التحيز وهو الصفة التابعة للحدوث والصادرة عن صفة الجوهرية بشرط الوجود . والرابعة الحصول في الحيز وهو الصفة المعللة بالمعنى . قالوا : وليس للجوهر الفرد صفة زائدة على هذه الأربع فليس له بكونه أسود وأبيض صفة وكذا القول فى كل عرض غير مشروط بالحياة . وأما الأعراض فالصفات العائدة إلى الجملة غير معقولة فيها وأما العائدة إلى الآحاد فعلته الصفة الحاصلة حالتى العدم والوجود والصفة الصادرة عنها عند الوجود صفة الوجود . فهذا هو المذهب الذي استقر جمهورهم عليه وهو قول أبي على وأبى هاشم والقاضى عبد الجبار وأنى رشيد وابن مثوبة . ومنهم من خالف هذا التفصيل في مواضع أحدها : أن أبا يعقوب والشحام وأبا عبد الله البصرى وأبا إسحاق بن عياش زعموا أن الجوهرية هي التحيز ثم اختلفوا بعد ذلك فزعم الشحام وأبو عبد الله أن
As for the Mu'tazilites, those among them who affirm non-existent essences agreed that all of them are equal in being essences, and that difference among them is only through attributes. Then they disagreed. The majority went to the view that they are qualified by genus-attributes, meaning that the essence of substances is qualified by the attribute of substantiality, the essence of blackness by the attribute of blackness, and so on. Ibn 'Ayyash claimed that those essences are bare of all attributes, and attributes are only attained at the time of existence. Those who affirm attributes claimed that the attributes of substances are either returning to the whole (al-jumla), which is life and all that is conditional upon it, or to individuals, which are either in substances or in accidents. As for substances, they affirmed four attributes for them: first, the attribute obtaining in both states of non-existence and existence, which is substantiality; second, existence, which is the attribute obtained from the agent; third, occupying space (tahayyuz), which is the attribute following upon origination and emanating from the attribute of substantiality under the condition of existence; fourth, being located in a place (al-husul fi al-hayyiz), which is the attribute caused by a meaning. They said: The individual substance has no attribute beyond these four, so it has no attribute of being black or white, and similarly for every accident not conditional upon life. As for accidents, attributes returning to the whole are not intelligible in them, and as for attributes returning to individuals, they have the attribute obtaining in both states of non-existence and existence, and the attribute emanating from it at existence is the attribute of existence. This is the doctrine upon which their majority settled, and it is the saying of Abu 'Ali, Abu Hashim, al-Qadi 'Abd al-Jabbar, Abu Rashid, and Ibn Mathwa. Some of them opposed this detail in certain respects. One of them: Abu Ya'qub, al-Shahham, Abu 'Abd Allah al-Basri, and Abu Ishaq ibn 'Ayyash claimed that substantiality is occupying space. Then they disagreed after that. Al-Shahham and Abu 'Abd Allah claimed that...
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage details the Mu'tazilite positions on whether non-existent essences have attributes. All Mu'tazilites who affirm non-existent essences agree that all essences are equal in being essences, and differences arise only through attributes. They then divided: the majority (jumhur) held that essences are qualified by genus-attributes (sifat al-ajnas), such as substantiality for substances and blackness for blackness. Ibn 'Ayyash held that essences are bare of all attributes until existence. The majority then provided a detailed classification of attributes for substances: four attributes: substantiality (obtains in both non-existence and existence), existence (from the agent), occupying space (follows origination), and being in a place (caused by a meaning). They denied other attributes like color. For accidents, only two attributes: the attribute obtaining in both states and the attribute of existence. This is the settled doctrine of the majority. Some dissented: al-Shahham and others identified substantiality with occupying space, but the text cuts off before completing their view.

Key Terms
الجوهرية
الوجود
التحيز
الحصول في الحيز
الجوهر الفرد
العرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How many attributes did the majority Mu'tazilites affirm for individual substances?
A) Two: existence and occupying space.
B) Four: substantiality, existence, occupying space, and being in a place.
C) Three: life, knowledge, and power.
D) Five: the above plus color.

Key Takeaway
The majority Mu'tazilites devised a precise four-attribute scheme for substances and two-attribute scheme for accidents, denying any extra attributes like color.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at an object's color, do you instinctively see it as an essential part of the object? How does the Mu'tazilite denial of color as an attribute of substance challenge your everyday perception of reality? Are you willing to see physical properties as mere illusions granted by Allah?
?
Existence is taught here as an attribute granted by the Agent (Allah). Does your daily dependence on material things reflect a belief that their existence is self-sufficient, or do you truly rely on Allah every moment for their continued existence? What would change if you acted on the latter?
?
The passage distinguishes between the attribute that remains whether the substance exists or not (substantiality) and the attribute given at creation (existence). How does this distinction affect your understanding of your own existence before Allah? Do you sense that your 'self' is something that persists even beyond death, or do you live as if death is total annihilation?

Dissenters: Al-Shahham and Substantiality as Occupying Space
Dissenting view equating substantiality with occupying space.

Core Idea
Some Mu'tazilites equated substantiality with occupying space, disputing the majority's separation.

While the majority settled on a detailed classification, a group of dissenters rejected that substantiality and occupying space are distinct attributes. Led by Abu Ya'qub, al-Shahham, Abu 'Abd Allah al-Basri, and Ibn 'Ayyash (the same who denied earlier), they claimed that substantiality is identical to occupying space. However, even among these dissenters, further disagreement arose, as al-Shahham and Abu 'Abd Allah then held differing views. This lesson examines this minority stance, though the text cuts off before completing their arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Note that this is the beginning of a list of dissenting points (the text breaks off).
2
Identify the dissenters
The passage names Abu Ya'qub, al-Shahham, Abu 'Abd Allah al-Basri, and Abu Ishaq ibn 'Ayyash as claiming that substantiality is occupying space.
Ibn 'Ayyash appears both here and earlier, but note the context may differ.
3
Understand their claim
They equate substantiality (jawhariyya) with occupying space (tahayyuz). This simplifies the attribute scheme, reducing the four attributes to three or fewer.
4
Note the subsequent disagreement
After this claim, al-Shahham and Abu 'Abd Allah then disagreed among themselves, but the text cuts off before detailing their further distinctions.

?

?

What would it mean for you to revisit a key concept you take for granted, and re-examine it in light of deeper study? Are you willing to let go of comfortable certainties for the sake of truth?

The Mu'tazilite View on Non-Existent Things as Essences
Razi lists Mu'tazilite scholars who held that non-existent possible things are real essences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite claim that non-existent possible things are eternal essences and that God's causation only brings them into existence, not creates their essence.

Core Idea
Non-existent possible things have real essences; God only makes them exist.

In this passage, Imam al-Razi reports the position of key Mu'tazilite theologians regarding the nature of non-existent things (al-ma'dumat). They argued that before coming into existence, possible entities already possess an essence (dhat), an individual identity, and a reality. This raises important questions about the nature of divine causation and the eternity of essences. Let's examine the precise claims and the points of agreement and disagreement among them.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the list of prominent Mu'tazilite scholars who held this view.
The passage is dense; reading both versions helps grasp the key terms.
2
Identify the central claim
The Mu'tazilites assert that non-existent possible things are essences, individuals, and realities before existence. The agent's role is not to create the essence but to make that essence existent.
This distinguishes between essence and existence.
3
Understand the disagreement
The scholars disagreed on whether these essences are distinct as individuals (ashkhas) in their state of non-existence.
Some held they are distinct, others not.
4
Note the agreement on infinite number
They agreed that for each type of non-existent thing, an infinite number of instances are established (thabit).
This implies an infinite realm of possibilities.
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider how this view challenges the idea of creation ex nihilo by positing eternal essences that are not brought into being from absolute nothingness.
This sets the stage for further discussion on divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
زعم أبو يعقوب الشحام وأبو علي الجبائي وابنه أبو هاشم وأبو الحسين بن الخياط وأبو عبد الله البصري وأبو إسحاق بن عياش والقاضي عبد الجبار بن أحمد وتلامذته أن المعدومات الممكنة قبل دخولها في الوجود ذوات وأعيان وحقائق وأن تأثير الفاعل ليس في جعلها ذوات بل في جعل تلك الذوات موجودة، واختلفوا على أن تلك الذوات متباينة بأشخاصها، واتفقوا على أن الثابت من كل نوع من تلك المعدومات عدد غير متناه.
Abu Ya'qub al-Shahham, Abu 'Ali al-Jubba'i, his son Abu Hashim, Abu al-Husayn ibn al-Khayyat, Abu 'Abd Allah al-Basri, Abu Ishaq ibn 'Ayyash, al-Qadi 'Abd al-Jabbar ibn Ahmad, and their students claimed that possible non-existent things before their entry into existence are essences (dhawat), individuals (a'yan), and realities (haqa'iq), and that the effect of the agent is not in making them essences but in making those essences existent. And they disagreed that those essences are distinct in their individuals, and they agreed that what is established from each type of those non-existent things is an infinite number.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage is a report of the Mu'tazilite position on non-existent things (ma'dumat). Razi lists the prominent Mu'tazilite figures who held that possible non-existents, before being brought into existence, are real essences (dhawat), individuals, and realities. They claim that the agent's (i.e., God's) causation does not create the essence itself but rather brings that pre-existing essence into existence. They disagreed on whether those essences are distinct as individuals (some said yes, some no), but agreed that each type of non-existent thing has an infinite number of instances established. This sets the stage for the subsequent detailed discussion of attributes.

Key Terms
معدوم
ذات
عين
حقيقة
فاعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think this view denies creation ex nihilo, meaning that God does not create anything.
Correction: The Mu'tazilites affirm that God creates existence, but they hold that the essences themselves are eternal concepts in the divine knowledge, not temporal creations.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazila mentioned, what is the role of the agent (God) regarding non-existent essences?
A) To create the essences themselves.
B) To make the essences existent, not to create them as essences.
C) To destroy the essences.
D) None of the above.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilites argued that possible non-existent things have real essences that are not created by God; rather, God makes those essences existent, while they disagreed on their distinctness but agreed on their infinite number.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God does not create your essence but only brings it into existence, would you feel less dependent on Him for who you are? Yet the Mu'tazilite view still holds that existence is a gift. How often do you thank God not only for your existence but for the very fact that you are something rather than nothing?
?
Consider the infinite number of possible essences that God never chose to bring into existence. Does that reality humble you about your own significance? How should it affect your trust in God's wisdom when you face trials?
?
The Mu'tazilites insisted that God's effect is not in making essences but in making them exist. Does your worship reflect that you owe God both your essence and your existence, or do you sometimes take your identity for granted as if it were self-sufficient?

Mu'tazila on Non-Existent Essences: Equality and Difference
Razi explains the Mu'tazilite agreement that all non-existent essences are equal in essence and differ only by attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite position that all non-existent essences are equal in essence and differ only by attributes, and the internal disagreement about whether they possess genus-attributes before existence.

Core Idea
Non-existent essences are equal in essence: difference comes from attributes, not essence.

Imam Razi presents a key Mu'tazilite doctrine: all non-existent essences share the same essence-hood, and any distinction between them arises from attributes, not from the essence itself. However, the Mu'tazila disagreed on whether these attributes exist in the state of non-existence. This passage lays out the majority view and the minority view of Ibn 'Ayyash.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and locate the agreement
Notice the first sentence: the Mu'tazila who affirm non-existent essences agree that all are equal in being essences, and difference is only by attributes. This is their core shared position.
2
Identify the two camps on attributes
The passage then presents a split: the majority holds that essences have genus-attributes (like substantiality for substances, blackness for black). Ibn 'Ayyash says no attributes at all until existence. Mark these two views.
3
Consider the implication
Reflect on what it means for an essence to have a genus-attribute in non-existence. Does that attribute define its kind? The commentary will clarify.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما المعتزلة فقد اتفق القائلون منهم بالذوات المعدومة على أنها بأسرها متساوية في كونها ذوات، وأن الاختلاف بينها ليس إلا بالصفات، ثم اختلفوا فذهب الجمهور منهم إلى أنها موصوفة بصفات الأجناس ومرادهم منها أن ذوات الجواهر موصوفة بصفة الجوهرية وذات السواد موصوفة بصفة السوادية وهلم جرا. وزعم ابن عياش أن تلك الذوات عارية عن جميع الصفات والصفات لا تحصل إلا زمان الوجود.
As for the Mu'tazila, those among them who affirm non-existent essences agreed that they are entirely equal in being essences, and that difference between them is only by attributes. Then they disagreed: the majority of them held that they are described by genus-attributes (sifat al-ajnas), meaning by that that the essences of substances are described by the attribute of substantiality (jawhariyya), the essence of blackness is described by the attribute of blackness (sawadiyya), and so on. And Ibn 'Ayyash claimed that those essences are devoid of all attributes, and attributes do not obtain except at the time of existence.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazilite view on non-existent essences. They agree that all such essences are equal in being essences (i.e., they share the same 'essence-hood'). The only difference between them comes from attributes, not from the essence itself. Then there is a split: the majority holds that each essence has a genus-attribute – e.g., a substance's essence has the attribute 'substantiality', blackness has 'blackness', etc. Ibn 'Ayyash disagrees, saying that essences have no attributes at all before existence; attributes only arise with existence. This sets up a key debate about whether attributes are essential or contingent.

Key Terms
ذات
صفة
جوهرية
سوادية
جنس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Genus-attributes imply that essences are different in essence, not just in attributes.
Correction: The passage states that all essences are 'equal in being essences', so genus-attributes are additional properties that do not affect the shared essence-hood.

Checkpoint
What is the position of Ibn 'Ayyash regarding attributes of non-existent essences?
A) They possess genus-attributes.
B) They are entirely devoid of attributes; attributes appear only at existence.
C) They have all attributes potentially.
D) They have only the attribute of existence.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila agree that all non-existent essences share the same essence, differing only by attributes; but they disagree on whether those attributes exist before creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If all non-existent essences are equal in their dhat, what does that say about the source of your own sense of worth? Is it based on attributes that come and go, or on something deeper that does not change? How does this shape your attachment to what you have or lack?
?
Ibn 'Ayyash says attributes only appear at existence. Think of something you strongly desire but do not yet possess—what attributes make it seem valuable? If those attributes are not real until it exists, are you chasing a fantasy? How does this change your urgency to acquire it?
?
The majority believe attributes are present even before existence. Consider a virtue you hope to have one day—does its essence already exist in you as a potentiality? What does that demand of you now, before that virtue manifests in action?
