Disagreement on Substantiality and Spatial Occupation
Razi presents the first disagreement on the relation of substantiality and spatial occupation for non-existent substances.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first disagreement among Mu'tazila on whether non-existent substances have spatial occupation and substantiality.

Core Idea
The nature of a non-existent substance divides the Mu'tazila.

This lesson examines the first point of contention: whether substantiality (jawhariyya) is identical to spatial occupation (tahayyuz). The Mu'tazilite scholars disagreed among themselves, leading to three distinct positions on the attributes of non-existent substances.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scholars and their shared claim
Note that Abu Ya'qub, al-Shahham, Abu 'Abd Allah al-Basri, and Ibn 'Ayyash all define substantiality as spatial occupation.
2
Distinguish al-Shahham's position
Al-Shahham held that a non-existent substance actually occupies place before existence.
3
Contrast Abu 'Abd Allah's position
He said the substance has the attribute of spatial occupation but is not actually in place until existence.
4
Understand Ibn 'Ayyash's radical conclusion
If spatial occupation is impossible in non-existence, then substantiality is also impossible, so essences have no attributes at all.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنهم من خالف هذا التفصيل في مواضع: أحدها: أن أبا يعقوب والشحام وأبا عبد الله البصري وأبا إسحاق بن عياش زعموا أن الجوهرية هي التحيز ثم اختلفوا بعد ذلك فزعم الشحام وأبو عبد الله أن ذات الجوهر كما أنها موصوفة بالجوهرية فهي موصوفة بالتحيز ثم اختلفا. فذهب الشحام إلى أن الجوهر حال عدمه حاصل في الحيز قبل الوجود، وذهب أبو عبد الله إلى أن الشرط في كون المتحيز حاصلا في الحيز هو الوجود، فإن الجوهر قبل وجوده موصوف بالتحيز ولكنه غير حاصل في الحيز. وزعم ابن عياش أن الجوهر حال العدم كما يمتنع اتصافه بالتحيز يمتنع اتصافه بالجوهرية فلهذا ثبت الذوات خالية عن الصفات. وثانيها: اختلفوا في أن المعدوم هل له بكونه معدوما صفة؟ فالكل أنكروه إلا أبا عبد الله البصري فإنه قال به. وثالثها: اتفقوا على أن الجواهر المعدومة لا توصف بأنها أجسام حال العدم إلا أبا الحسين الخياط فإنه قال به. ورابعها: أنهم [اتفقوا] على أن بعد العلم بأن للعالم صانعا عالما قادرا حيا حكيما مرسلا للرسل يمكننا الشك في أنه هل هو موجود أولا إلى أن يعرف ذلك بالدليل، لأنهم لما جوزوا اتصاف المعدوم بالصفة لم يلزم من اتصاف ذات الله تعالى بصفة العالمية والقادرية كونه موجودا فلا بد من دلالة منفصلة. واتفق الباقون من العقلاء على أن ذلك جهالة وإلا لزم أن لا يعرف وجود الأجسام المتحركة والساكنة إلا بالدليل وبالله التوفيق.
And among them are those who disagreed with this detailed classification in places: First: That Abu Ya'qub, al-Shahham, Abu 'Abd Allah al-Basri, and Abu Ishaq ibn 'Ayyash claimed that substantiality (jawhariyya) is spatial occupation (tahayyuz). Then they disagreed after that: al-Shahham and Abu 'Abd Allah claimed that the essence of a substance, just as it is described by substantiality, so it is described by spatial occupation, then they diverged: al-Shahham held that the substance in its state of non-existence is occupying place (hasil fi al-hayyiz) before existence, and Abu 'Abd Allah held that the condition for the spatial occupant to be in place is existence, so the substance before its existence is described by spatial occupation but is not actually in place. And Ibn 'Ayyash claimed that the substance in the state of non-existence, just as it is impossible for it to be described by spatial occupation, it is impossible for it to be described by substantiality; therefore essences are established as devoid of attributes. Second: They disagreed on whether the non-existent has an attribute by being non-existent. All denied it except Abu 'Abd Allah al-Basri, who affirmed it. Third: They agreed that non-existent substances are not described as bodies in the state of non-existence, except Abu al-Husayn al-Khayyat, who affirmed it. Fourth: They [agreed] that after knowing that the world has a Maker, knowing, powerful, living, wise, sender of messengers, we can doubt whether He is existent or not until that is known by the proof, because since they allowed the non-existent to be described by an attribute, it does not follow from the description of God's essence by the attributes of knowledge and power that He is existent, so a separate proof is needed. And the rest of the rational people agreed that this is ignorance, otherwise it would follow that the existence of moving and stationary bodies would not be known except by proof. And with God is success.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage lists four specific points of disagreement among the Mu'tazilites regarding non-existent substances. First: Some (Abu Ya'qub, al-Shahham, Abu 'Abd Allah, Ibn 'Ayyash) identify substantiality with spatial occupation. But then they split: al-Shahham says a non-existent substance actually occupies place; Abu 'Abd Allah says it has the attribute of spatial occupation but is not actually in place until existence; Ibn 'Ayyash says that if spatial occupation is impossible in non-existence, so is substantiality, hence no attributes at all. Second: Whether 'being non-existent' itself is an attribute. Most deny it; Abu 'Abd Allah affirms. Third: Whether non-existent substances can be described as bodies. Most say no; only Abu al-Husayn al-Khayyat says yes. Fourth: A logical consequence: if non-existents can have attributes (like knowledge, power), then from God's attributes of knowledge and power we cannot infer His existence without a separate proof. The majority of rational people consider this position absurd, as it would require a proof even for the existence of physical bodies.

Key Terms
جوهرية
تحيز
حيز
معدوم
ذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn 'Ayyash, what is the status of a non-existent substance's attributes?
A) It has substantiality but not spatial occupation.
B) It has both attributes.
C) It has neither attribute.
D) It has spatial occupation but not substantiality.

Key Takeaway
The first disagreement shows that Mu'tazilites could not agree on whether non-existent substances have spatial occupation or substantiality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter disagreements among respected scholars about something as fundamental as the attributes of non-existent things, does it unsettle your confidence in human reasoning about the unseen? How do you personally handle theological uncertainty without either dismissing it or letting it weaken your faith?
?
This passage shows scholars making fine distinctions about what can be attributed to a non-existent substance. Are there areas in your own life where you insist on precise definitions or labels before you are willing to act or believe? Could that precision be a barrier to humility before Allah's absolute knowledge?

Disagreement on Non-Existence as an Attribute
Razi presents the second disagreement on whether non-existent things have the attribute of non-existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second disagreement among Mu'tazila: whether the state of being non-existent can be considered an attribute.

Core Idea
Is 'non-existence' an attribute? Most said no, except Abu 'Abd Allah al-Basri.

The second point of contention is straightforward: can 'being non-existent' itself be described as an attribute of a thing? Most Mu'tazilites denied this, but Abu 'Abd Allah al-Basri affirmed it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
It states that all denied that the non-existent has an attribute by being non-existent except Abu 'Abd Allah al-Basri.
2
Reflect on the implication
If non-existence is an attribute, it would allow attributes to exist without a subject, which is problematic in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that if a thing is non-existent, it cannot have any attributes at all.
Correction: In this disagreement, the issue is specifically whether 'non-existence' itself is an attribute; Abu 'Abd Allah says yes, but others say no. It does not deny that a non-existent can be described by other attributes (like being possible).

?

?
When you look back at your past sins—things that no longer exist in the present—do you still treat them as if they have a power over you? How does the majority's denial of non-existence as an attribute challenge you to stop defining yourself by what no longer exists?

Disagreement on Non-Existent Substances as Bodies
Razi presents the third disagreement on describing non-existent substances as bodies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third disagreement: whether non-existent substances are considered bodies.

Core Idea
Can a non-existent substance be called a body? Almost all said no, except Abu al-Husayn al-Khayyat.

The third point addresses the description of non-existent substances as 'bodies'. The overwhelming majority denied that substances in the state of non-existence can be called bodies, with only Abu al-Husayn al-Khayyat arguing otherwise.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
It says they agreed that non-existent substances are not described as bodies except Abu al-Husayn al-Khayyat.
2
Consider the significance
If a non-existent substance is not a body, then 'body' is an attribute that only appears upon existence.

Source Text

Disagreement on Implications for God's Existence
Razi presents the fourth disagreement on the logical consequence regarding God's existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Mu'tazilite position on attributes of non-existents leads to a skeptical challenge about God's existence.

Core Idea
If non-existents can have attributes, God's attributes do not prove His existence without a separate proof.

The fourth disagreement draws out a radical consequence: if non-existent things can possess attributes (like knowledge and power), then from God's attributes of knowledge and power we cannot infer that He exists. This leads to skepticism, which the majority of rational people considered ignorance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
It says that after knowing God's attributes, one can still doubt His existence because attributes do not entail existence for non-existents.
2
Understand the reasoning
The Mu'tazila who allowed attributes for non-existents (like Abu 'Abd Allah) would say that God's essence could have attributes without being existent.
3
Note the majority refutation
The rest of rational people say this is ignorance, because it would require a proof even for the existence of observable bodies.

Source Text

But then they split: al-Shahham says a non-existent substance actually occupies place; Abu 'Abd Allah says it has the attribute of spatial occupation but is not actually in place until existence; Ibn 'Ayyash says that if spatial occupation is impossible in non-existence, so is substantiality, hence no attributes at all. Second: Whether 'being non-existent' itself is an attribute. Most deny it; Abu 'Abd Allah affirms. Third: Whether non-existent substances can be described as bodies. Most say no; only Abu al-Husayn al-Khayyat says yes. Fourth: A logical consequence: if non-existents can have attributes (like knowledge, power), then from God's attributes of knowledge and power we cannot infer His existence without a separate proof. The majority of rational people consider this position absurd, as it would require a proof even for the existence of physical bodies.

Key Terms
صانع
عالم
قادر
دليل
جهالة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that if we know God's attributes, we automatically know He exists.
Correction: The passage argues that if non-existents can have attributes, then God's attributes alone do not prove existence; a separate argument is needed.

Checkpoint
According to the fourth disagreement, why might someone doubt God's existence after knowing His attributes?
A) Because God's attributes are contradictory.
B) Because non-existents can have attributes, so having attributes does not imply existence.
C) Because the world does not need a Maker.
D) Because existence is not an attribute.

Key Takeaway
The fourth disagreement shows that allowing attributes for non-existents leads to skepticism about God's existence, rejected by the majority.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever allowed a theological assumption to lead you into doubt about God's existence? What did you do with that doubt?
?
The majority dismissed this argument as ignorance. Is there an area of your faith where you have dismissed a nagging doubt without rigorously addressing it? How can you confront it honestly today?
?
This lesson forces you to consider what proof you personally rely on for God's existence. Is that proof strong enough to withstand the logical challenges raised here? What would it take to make it unshakeable?

The Harranians' Five Eternal Entities
Razi recounts the Harranian doctrine of five eternal entities with their arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Harranian doctrine of five eternal entities and the arguments they used to support each.

Core Idea
The Harranians believed in five eternal entities: Creator, soul, matter, time, and destiny.

Imam Razi presents the doctrine of the Harranians, a philosophical school that asserted the eternity of five principles. This lesson explores their classification and the reasoning behind each eternal entity. Understanding this view is important for grasping later refutations in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Focus on the classification of the five entities and the arguments for each.
The passage is dense with philosophical terminology.
2
Identify the five entities and their roles
Notice that the Harranians divide the five into two living agents (Creator and soul), one passive (hyle), and two neither active nor passive (time and destiny). Understand each role.
'Soul' here refers to the principle of life in humans and celestial beings.
3
Examine the arguments for eternity
For soul and hyle, the argument is based on infinite regress: everything originated needs matter, so if they were originated, an infinite chain would result. For time, they argue that assuming its non-existence is contradictory. For destiny, they claim it cannot be removed without losing spatial distinctions.
The argument for the Creator is said to be well-known and not detailed here.
4
Note the narrator's comment
Razi notes that this is a narration of their school, and that Abu Bakr al-Razi (the physician) adopted this view and wrote a book on it. He promises a later discussion.
This is not an endorsement; Razi will refute these ideas later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الحريانيون فقد أثبتوا خمسا من القدماء حيان فاعلان البارى والنفس ، وعنوا بالنفس ما يكون مبدأ للحياة وهى الأرواح البشرية والسماوية ، وواحد منفعل وهو الهيولى ، واثنان لا حيان ولا فاعلان ولا منفعلان وهما الدهر والقضاء . أما قدم البارى تعالى فالدليل عليه مشهور ، وأما قدم النفس والهيولى فهو بناء على أن كل محدث مسبوق بمادة فقالوا لو كانت النفس حادثة لكانت لها مادة ومادتها إن كانت حادثة افتقرت إلى مادة أخرى لا إلى نهاية ولزم التسلسل ، وإن كانت قديمة فهو المطلوب ، وأما الهيولى فإن كانت حادثة لزم التسلسل وإن كانت قديمة فهو المطلوب ، وأما الدهر وهو الزمان فلأنه غير قابل للعدم ، لأن كل ما يصح عليه العدم كان عدمه بعد وجوده بعدية زمانية فيكون الزمان موجوداً حال ما فرض معدوماً فهذا محال ، فإذا قد لزم من فرض عدمه لذاته محال فيكون واجباً لذاته ، وأما القضاء فهو أيضاً واجب لذاته لأن الواجب لذاته الذي يشهد صريح الفطارة [؟] بامتناع ارتفاعه ، والقضاء كذلك لأنه لو ارتفعت لما بقيت الجهات متميزة بحسب الإشارات وذلك غير معقول (۱) . (1) أقول : هذه حكاية مذهبهم وما يصلح لأن تكون دلائلهم عليه ، ومال ابن زكريا الطبيب الرازي إلى ذلك المذهب وعمل فيه كتابا موسوماً ، بالقول فى القدماء الخمسة ، وسيأتى القول في كل واحد منها .
As for the Harranians, they affirmed five of the eternal entities: two living, two agents: the Creator and the soul. By 'soul' they meant that which is the principle of life, which is the human and celestial souls. And one passive: the hyle (prime matter). And two neither living, nor agents, nor passive: time and destiny. As for the eternity of the Creator, the proof for it is well known. As for the eternity of the soul and the hyle, it is based on the [principle] that every originated thing is preceded by matter. They said: If the soul were originated, it would have a matter; if its matter were originated, it would need another matter ad infinitum, and infinite regress would follow; if it were eternal, that is the desired conclusion. As for the hyle: if it were originated, infinite regress would follow; if it were eternal, that is the desired conclusion. As for time (dahr), which is time (zaman), it is because it is not susceptible to non-existence, because everything that non-existence is possible for it, its non-existence after its existence is a temporal posteriority, so time would exist at the moment it is assumed non-existent — this is impossible. So if the assumption of its non-existence entails an impossibility, then it is necessary by itself. As for destiny (qada'), it is also necessary by itself, because the necessary by itself is that which the explicit innate disposition testifies to the impossibility of its removal, and destiny is likewise because if it were removed, the directions would not remain distinct according to spatial indications, and that is inconceivable (1). (1) I say: This is a narration of their school and what may serve as their proofs for it. And Ibn Zakariyya the physician al-Razi inclined to that school and wrote a book entitled 'The Statement on the Five Eternal Entities'. The discussion on each of them will come later.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage explains the doctrine of the Harraniyyun (a group of philosophers, possibly Sabians or certain Greek-influenced thinkers) regarding five eternal entities. Razi lists them: two are living agents (the Creator and the soul), one passive (hyle or prime matter), and two are neither living nor agents nor passive (time and destiny). He then presents the arguments they use for each. The eternity of the Creator is taken as well-known. For the soul and hyle, they argue from the principle that every originated thing is preceded by matter. If the soul were originated, it would need a matter; if that matter were originated, an infinite regress would follow. Therefore, the matter (or the soul itself) must be eternal. Similarly for hyle. For time (dahr/zaman), they argue that time cannot become non-existent because assuming non-existence of time would require time to exist at the moment of assumed non-existence, which is contradictory. Hence time is necessary by itself. For destiny (qada'), they claim it is necessary by itself because innate disposition testifies that its removal is impossible; otherwise directions would not be distinct. Razi then notes that this is a narration of their school and their proofs, and that the physician al-Razi (Abu Bakr al-Razi) adopted this view and wrote a book on the five eternal entities. Razi promises a later discussion of each.

الحريانيون
القدماء الخمسة
النفس
الهيولى
الدهر
القضاء
التسلسل
واجب لذاته

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think this doctrine represents Islamic theology.
Correction: This is a philosophical view held by the Harranians, not part of orthodox Islam. Imam Razi reports it for refutation later.

Checkpoint
What is the Harranian argument for the eternity of time (al-dahr)?
A) Time is necessary because it cannot be originated from nothing.
B) Time cannot be non-existent because assuming its non-existence would require time to exist at that moment, which is impossible.
C) Time is eternal because everything originated requires time.
D) Time is the same as destiny and both are necessary.

Key Takeaway
The Harranians attempted to prove the eternity of five entities using arguments from infinite regress and necessity, but their doctrine was later refuted by Islamic theologians.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Harranians believed that time is eternal and cannot cease. But the Quran says that on the Day of Judgment, the heavens will be rolled up like a scroll. How does your daily schedule reflect a belief that time is a loan from Allah, not a permanent reality? What one change would show that you truly believe time will end?
?
The Harranians argued that the soul is eternal. But you know that your soul was created and will return to its Creator. Are you living as if your soul is self-sufficient, or are you urgently preparing for the meeting with Allah? What fear holds you back from complete submission?
?
They claimed that destiny is necessary and cannot be removed. But how does that comfort you when you face trials? Or does it make you passive? What would it mean for you to truly rely on Allah's decree while still striving with all your strength?

The Harranian Doctrine of Five Eternals
Razi describes the Harranians' five eternals and their proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Harranian belief in five eternal entities and the rational arguments they advanced for their eternity.

Core Idea
The Harranians claimed five distinct eternal principles beyond the One Creator.

The Harranians (likely Hermetic Sabians) proposed a metaphysical system with multiple eternal beings. Razi presents their classification and proofs, showing how they reasoned for the eternity of soul, matter, time, and destiny alongside the Creator. This lesson surveys their doctrine and its justification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the five eternals
Read the first part of the passage where Razi lists the five entities and their categories (active living, passive, and neutral). Notice how the Creator and soul are active, matter is passive, and time and destiny are neither.
The soul is defined as the principle of life, including human and celestial spirits.
2
Examine the proof for soul and matter
Analyze the regress argument: if soul or matter were originated, they would need matter, leading to infinite regress unless the matter is eternal. Understand that they argue from the impossibility of infinite regress.
They assume every originated thing has matter, which is a metaphysical principle.
3
Understand the proof for time (dahr)
Read the argument that time cannot admit non-existence because its non-existence would be temporally posterior, implying time existing at that moment. This shows time is necessary.
Time here is considered a necessary being.
4
Grasp the proof for destiny (qada')
The argument states that destiny is necessary because its removal would make spatial distinctions impossible. Reflect on why spatial distinctions depend on destiny.
This links destiny to the structure of space.
5
Note Razi's editorial remark
Razi clarifies that this is an account of their doctrine and mentions that Ibn Zakariyya al-Razi (the physician) endorsed it. Consider the significance of a Muslim philosopher adopting this view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الحريانيون فقد أثبتوا خمسا من القدماء حيان فاعلان البارى والنفس ، وعنوا بالنفس ما يكون مبدأ للحياة وهى الأرواح البشرية والسماوية ، وواحد منفعل وهو الهيولى ، واثنان لا حيان ولا فاعلان ولا منفعلان وهما الدهر والقضاء . أما قدم البارى تعالى فالدليل عليه مشهور ، وأما قدم النفس والهيولى فهو بناء على أن كل محدث مسبوق بمادة فقالوا لو كانت النفس حادثة لكانت لها مادة ومادتها إن كانت حادثة افتقرت إلى مادة أخرى لا إلى نهاية ولزم التسلسل ، وإن كانت قديمة فهو المطلوب ، وأما الهيولى فإن كانت حادثة لزم التسلسل وإن كانت قديمة فهو المطلوب ، وأما الدهر وهو الزمان فلأنه غير قابل للعدم ، لأن كل ما يصح عليه العدم كان عدمه بعد وجوده بعدية زمانية فيكون الزمان موجوداً حال ما فرض معدوماً فهذا محال ، فإذا قد لزم من فرض عدمه لذاته محال فيكون واجباً لذاته ، وأما القضاء فهو أيضاً واجب لذاته لأن الواجب لذاته الذي يشهد صريح الفطارة بامتناع ارتفاعه ، والقضاء كذلك لأنه لو ارتفعت لما بقيت الجهات متميزة بحسب الإشارات وذلك غير معقول . أقول: هذه حكاية مذهبهم وما يصلح لأن تكون دلائلهم عليه ، ومال ابن زكريا الطبيب الرازي إلى ذلك المذهب وعمل فيه كتابا موسوماً ، بالقول فى القدماء الخمسة ، وسيأتى القول في كل واحد منها .
As for the Harranians, they affirmed five of the eternals: two active living beings: the Creator and the soul – and by 'soul' they meant that which is the principle of life, namely human and celestial spirits – and one passive being, which is matter (hayula), and two that are neither living, nor active, nor passive: time (dahr) and destiny (qada'). As for the eternity of the Creator Most High, the proof for it is well-known. As for the eternity of the soul and matter, it is based on the claim that every originated thing is preceded by matter. They said: if the soul were originated, it would have a matter, and if its matter were originated it would need another matter ad infinitum, leading to an infinite regress, and if it were eternal, that is the desired conclusion. As for matter, if it were originated, regress follows; if it were eternal, that is the desired conclusion. As for time (dahr), which is time (zaman), it is not receptive to non-existence, because everything to which non-existence applies, its non-existence after its existence is temporally posterior, so time would exist at the very moment it is assumed non-existent, which is impossible. Thus, since assuming its non-existence necessarily entails an impossibility, it is necessary by itself. As for destiny (qada'), it too is necessary by itself, because the necessary by itself is that whose removal is impossible according to the clear indication of primordial nature (fitrah), and destiny is likewise, because if it were removed, the directions would not remain distinct in terms of spatial indications, and that is inconceivable. I say: This is an account of their doctrine and what might serve as their proofs for it. And Ibn Zakariyya, the physician al-Razi, inclined to that doctrine and composed a book entitled 'The Discourse on the Five Eternals', and the discussion of each of them will come later.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage is an exposition of the doctrine of the Harranians (possibly Hermetics or Sabians) regarding five eternal entities. Razi first lists them: two active living beings—the Creator and the soul (defined as the principle of life, including human and celestial spirits); one passive being—matter (hylic matter); and two that are neither living, active, nor passive—time (dahr) and destiny (qada'). Then he presents their proofs for the eternity of each. For the Creator, the proof is well-known (not elaborated here). For the soul and matter, the proof is based on the regress argument: if they were originated, they would require matter, leading to infinite regress unless the matter is eternal. For time, the proof is that it cannot admit non-existence because any non-existence of time would itself be temporally posterior, leading to a contradiction. For destiny, it is necessary because its removal would obliterate spatial distinctions, which is inconceivable. Razi then adds an editorial note (أقول) stating that this is an account of their doctrine and that Ibn Zakariyya al-Razi (the physician, not the theologian) adhered to this view and wrote a book on the five eternals, promising further discussion. The passage thus presents a complete summary of the Harranian position and its justifications.

القدماء
النفس
الهيولى
الدهر
القضاء
التسلسل
العَدَم

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the Harranians denied the Creator's eternity, but they affirmed it along with four other eternals. Also, time and destiny might be considered active, but they are neither living nor active in their view.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that the Harranians affirmed five eternals including the Creator, and classifies time and destiny as neither living, active, nor passive.

Checkpoint
According to the Harranians, how many eternal entities did they affirm?
A) Three: Creator, soul, and matter
B) Four: Creator, soul, matter, and time
C) Five: Creator, soul, matter, time, and destiny
D) Two: Creator and soul

Key Takeaway
The Harranians affirmed five eternal principles, each with a distinct rational argument for its necessity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Harranians argued that time (dahr) is eternal because its non-existence leads to contradiction. Do you live as though your time is limited and accountable to the Creator, or do you treat it as if it were self-subsisting and unaccountable? What concrete change would you make today to realign your perspective?
?
The Harranians claimed the soul is eternal. The Quran teaches that the soul is created and returns to its Lord. Which voice do you listen to more often—the one that makes the soul independent and self-sufficient, or the one that reminds you the soul stands before its Maker? What can you do to let the Quran's voice dominate?
?
The Harranians attached destiny to an impersonal principle. The Islamic understanding ties destiny to Allah's will and wisdom. Have you ever liked your own plan over what Allah decreed for you? Take a moment to repent and affirm that His decree is better than any alternative.

The Ash'ari Thesis: Accidents Cannot Endure
Razi states the Ash'ari consensus and the first proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari position that accidents cannot endure and their first rational proof.

Core Idea
Accidents cannot survive because survival would require an accident to subsist in an accident.

Imam Razi begins by stating the unanimous Ash'ari doctrine that accidents are momentary. He then provides a concise proof: if an accident were to endure, its survival would be an accident itself, leading to an infinite regress of accidents subsisting in accidents. This lesson lays the foundation for the detailed arguments that follow.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage once
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the thesis without analysis.
2
Identify the proof's structure
Notice that the proof is based on defining survival as an attribute (accident).
3
Reflect on the consequence
Ask why accident-on-accident is impossible in the Ash'ari framework.
This connects to the broader ontology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : اتفقت الأشاعرة على امتناع بقاء العرض لأن البقاء صفة فلو بقى العرض لزم قيام العرض بالعرض ولأنه لو صح بقاء العرض لامتنع عدمه ، لأن عدمه بعد البقاء لا يجوز أن يكون واجباً وإلا لانقلب الشيء من الإمكان الذاتي إلى الامتناع ، بل يكون جائزا وله سبب وهو إما وجودى أو عدمى ، أما الوجود فإما الموجب كما يقال إنه يفنى الطريان الصد محال ، لأن طريان الضد على المحل مشروط بعدم الضد الأول عنه ، فلو عمل ذلك العدم به لزم الدور ، وإما المختار كما يقال الله تعالى بعدمه وهو محال لأن المعدم حين الإعدام إما أن يكون قد صدر عنه أمر أو لم يصدر ، فإن صدر عنه أمر فتأثير فى تحصيل أمر وجودى فهذا يكون إيجاداً لا إعداماً ، وإن لم يصدر عنه أمر فهو محال لأن القادر لا بد له من أثر ، وأما العدمي فإنه ينتفى لانتفاء شرطه لكن شرطه الجوهر وهو باق والكلام في كيفية عدمه كالكلام فى كيفية عدم العرض فثبت أنه لو صح بقاؤه لامتنع عدمه ، لكنه قد يعدم لامتنع بقاؤه فقيل على الأول لا نسلم أن البقاء عرض سلمناه لكن لم لا يجوز قيام مثل هذا العرض بالعرض ، وعلى الثانى لم لا يجوز أن يجب عدمه بعد بقائه في زمان معين ، وهذا لأن العرض عندكم كان جائز الوجود فى الزمان الأول ثم انقلب ممتنعا في الزمان الثانى فلم لا يجوز أن يبقى أزمنة كثيرة ثم ينتهي إلى زمان يصير فيه ممتنع الوجود بعينه وحينئذ يفنى لا لسبب سلمنا أنه لابد له من سبب لكن لم لا يجوز أن ينتفى لانتفاء الشرط وهو أن تكون الأعراض الباقية مشروطة بأعراض لا تبقى فعند انقطاعها يفنى الباقى ، ولا يبقى في دفع هذا الاحتمال إلا الاستقراء الذى لا يفيد إلا الظن، ثم احتجوا على جواز بقائها بأنها لو كانت مستكنة الوجود فى الزمان الأول وتكون كذلك فى الزمان الثانى إذ لو جاز أن ينقلب الممكن لذاته في زمان ممتنعا في زمان آخر لجاز أن ينقلب الممتنع في زمان واجبا في زمان آخر ، وعلى هذا يجوز أن يكون العالم قبل وجوده ممتنعا الوجود لعينه ثم انقلب واجبا لعينه ، وعلى هذا التقدير يلزم نفى الصانع تعالى عنه علوا كبيرا . جو هو جزء من مفهوم السوادية أن السواد مما يتيقنه البصر واللون أحق بأن يكون صفة ، والجنس لا يكون عرضا قائما بالنوع ولا الجزء به يكل ، وأيضاً كون العرض حالا في محله إضافة لا وجود لها إلا في العقل كما ولا يتسلسل بل تقف عند وقوف العقل عن الاعتبار وكون الحلول نقيضاً للاحلول لا يقتضى وجود الحلول كما بيناه مرارا وحوالة الجواب على ما مر غير مفيد ههنا. والقائلون به يقولون كل عرض يحل في محل فإنه يفيد صفة المحل، والسرعة تجعل الحركة سريعة ولا يوصف الجسم بها فهو عرض للحركة لا للجسم ، والوحدة إن كانت عرضا فوحدة العرض تحل فيه والنقطة فصل للخط لا للجسم . أقول : أبو الحسين البصرى يدعى أن العلم ببقاء الأعراض كالسواد والبياض ضرورى . وقوله : بأن طرو الضد على المحل مشروط بعدم الضد الأول دعوى مجردة لا يقبلها القائل بأن الضد ينتفى عند طريان ضده ، بل يقول عدم الضد الأول معلل بطريان الضد على محله وترجيح أحد القولين على الآخر محتاج إلى دليل . وقوله : المعدم إن صدر عنه أمر فتأثيره في تحصيل أمر وجودى أيضا غير مسلم عنده ، فإنه يقول تأثيره أمر متجدد وذلك الأمر ليس إيجاد معدوم بل هو إعدام موجود ، ما الدليل على أن الأول ممكن وحده دونه
Issue: The Ash'aris have agreed on the impossibility of the survival of an accident, because survival is an attribute; so if an accident survived, subsistence of an accident in an accident would follow. And because if the survival of an accident were valid, its non-existence would be impossible; for its non-existence after survival cannot be necessary, otherwise a thing would change from essential possibility to impossibility, rather it is possible and has a cause, which is either existential or non-existential. As for the existential, it is either necessitating, as it is said that it annihilates by the occurrence of the opposite, but that is impossible because the occurrence of the opposite in a substratum is conditioned by the non-existence of the first opposite from it; if that non-existence were brought about by it, circularity would follow. Or it is voluntary, as it is said that God annihilates it, but that is impossible because the annihilator at the time of annihilation either issued an act or not; if an act issued, then it is an effect in producing an existential thing, so that would be creation, not annihilation; if no act issued, it is impossible because the powerful must have an effect. As for the non-existential cause, it is removed by the removal of its condition, but its condition is the substance which remains, and the discussion about how it becomes non-existent is like the discussion about how the accident becomes non-existent. So it is established that if its survival were valid, its non-existence would be impossible. Yet it may become non-existent, so survival is impossible. It was said: for the first, we do not grant that survival is an accident; if we grant it, why is it not permissible for such an accident to subsist in an accident? For the second, why is it not permissible for its non-existence to become necessary after its survival in a specific time? This is because the accident, according to you, was possible in the first time, then became impossible in the second time; so why is it not permissible for it to survive many times, then come to a time in which it becomes impossible in itself, and then it perishes without a cause? If we grant that it must have a cause, why is it not permissible for it to be removed by the removal of a condition, namely that the enduring accidents are conditioned by accidents that do not endure, so when they cease, the enduring perishes? There remains no way to refute this except induction, which yields only conjecture. Then they argued for the permissibility of their survival: if they were stable in existence in the first time and are also so in the second time, then if it is possible for the possible in itself to become impossible in another time, it would also be possible for the impossible to become necessary in another time. On this basis, it would be possible for the world before its existence to be impossible in itself, then to become necessary in itself, and on this estimation, the negation of the Creator would follow - exalted is He above that greatly. And is part of the concept of blackness that blackness is perceived by sight, and color is more deserving of being an attribute, and the genus is not an accident subsisting in the species nor the part in it, and also that an accident being in its substratum is a relation that has no existence except in the mind, and it does not regress infinitely but stops when the mind stops considering, and that inherence being the opposite of non-inherence does not necessitate the existence of inherence, as we have explained repeatedly, and referring the answer to what has passed is not useful here. Those who hold this say: every accident that inheres in a substratum imparts an attribute upon the substratum; speed makes motion fast and the body is not described by it, so it is an accident of motion, not of the body; and unity, if it is an accident, then the unity of the accident inheres in it; and the point is a division of the line, not of the body. I say: Abu al-Husayn al-Basri claims that knowledge of the survival of accidents like blackness and whiteness is necessary. His statement that the occurrence of the opposite in a substratum is conditioned by the non-existence of the first opposite is a mere claim, not accepted by those who say that the opposite is removed by the occurrence of its opposite; rather, they say the non-existence of the first opposite is caused by the occurrence of the opposite in its substratum, and preferring one of the two statements over the other requires evidence. Also his statement that the annihilator, if an act issues from him, his effect is in producing an existential thing is not granted by him, for he says his effect is a renewed thing, and that thing is not the creation of a non-existent but the annihilation of an existent. What is the evidence that the first is possible and not the second?
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage presents the Ash'ari position that accidents cannot endure for more than one moment. The argument is that if an accident could survive, it would require another accident (survival) to subsist in it, leading to accident-on-accident. Moreover, if survival were possible, then its non-existence would be impossible, leading to absurd consequences. The passage then examines possible causes for the non-existence of an enduring accident and refutes them. It also gives a proof from the philosophers that if accidents could be contingent at one time and impossible at another, then the world might have been impossible before creation, leading to atheism. The footnote by Razi criticizes Abu al-Husayn al-Basri's claim that knowledge of the survival of accidents is intuitive, and challenges his arguments about the conditions for the occurrence of opposites and about the nature of annihilation. The passage covers a wide range of arguments and responses.

Key Terms
بقاء
عرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Ash'aris deny the reality of accidents.
Correction: The Ash'aris affirm accidents exist but only for an instant; they are continually recreated by God.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why can an accident not endure?
A) Because survival is an accident that would subsist in the original accident.
B) Because accidents are not real.
C) Because substances prevent them.
D) Because God annihilates them.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari proof against accident endurance hinges on the impossibility of one accident subsisting in another.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every accident in your life—your breath, your sight, your very thoughts—is recreated by Allah each instant, then what is your excuse for not turning to Him in every moment? Do you truly feel that you have no independent power? When was the last time you acted as if you couldn't take the next breath without His creation?
?
The Ash'ari proof shows that if an accident were to endure, an impossible scenario would follow. What 'impossible' scenarios in your own heart—like thinking you can accumulate good deeds without divine creation each moment—are you still holding onto? Will you renounce them today and live fully dependent on Allah?

Sects and Disagreements
intermediate
5 min
The Second Proof: Survival Leads to Impossibility of Annihilation
Razi presents the second proof and the need for a cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that if accidents could endure, their non-existence would be impossible, leading to a contradiction.

Core Idea
If an accident could survive, its annulment would be impossible without absurd consequences.

The Ash'ari argument proceeds with a second proof: if an accident were to survive, its subsequent non-existence could not be necessary (otherwise it would change from possible to impossible). Rather, it would be contingent and require a cause, which is either existential or non-existential. This lesson introduces the logical dilemma.

Guided Path
1
Read the conditional
Understand the phrase: 'if survival possible, then non-existence impossible'.
2
Identify the three possibilities
The non-existence could be necessary, impossible, or contingent.
3
Note the conclusion
Non-existence must be contingent and therefore have a cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : اتفقت الأشاعرة على امتناع بقاء العرض لأن البقاء صفة فلو بقى العرض لزم قيام العرض بالعرض ولأنه لو صح بقاء العرض لامتنع عدمه ، لأن عدمه بعد البقاء لا يجوز أن يكون واجباً وإلا لانقلب الشيء من الإمكان الذاتي إلى الامتناع ، بل يكون جائزا وله سبب وهو إما وجودى أو عدمى ، أما الوجود فإما الموجب كما يقال إنه يفنى الطريان الصد محال ، لأن طريان الضد على المحل مشروط بعدم الضد الأول عنه ، فلو عمل ذلك العدم به لزم الدور ، وإما المختار كما يقال الله تعالى بعدمه وهو محال لأن المعدم حين الإعدام إما أن يكون قد صدر عنه أمر أو لم يصدر ، فإن صدر عنه أمر فتأثير فى تحصيل أمر وجودى فهذا يكون إيجاداً لا إعداماً ، وإن لم يصدر عنه أمر فهو محال لأن القادر لا بد له من أثر ، وأما العدمي فإنه ينتفى لانتفاء شرطه لكن شرطه الجوهر وهو باق والكلام في كيفية عدمه كالكلام فى كيفية عدم العرض فثبت أنه لو صح بقاؤه لامتنع عدمه ، لكنه قد يعدم لامتنع بقاؤه فقيل على الأول لا نسلم أن البقاء عرض سلمناه لكن لم لا يجوز قيام مثل هذا العرض بالعرض ، وعلى الثانى لم لا يجوز أن يجب عدمه بعد بقائه في زمان معين ، وهذا لأن العرض عندكم كان جائز الوجود فى الزمان الأول ثم انقلب ممتنعا في الزمان الثانى فلم لا يجوز أن يبقى أزمنة كثيرة ثم ينتهي إلى زمان يصير فيه ممتنع الوجود بعينه وحينئذ يفنى لا لسبب سلمنا أنه لابد له من سبب لكن لم لا يجوز أن ينتفى لانتفاء الشرط وهو أن تكون الأعراض الباقية مشروطة بأعراض لا تبقى فعند انقطاعها يفنى الباقى ، ولا يبقى في دفع هذا الاحتمال إلا الاستقراء الذى لا يفيد إلا الظن، ثم احتجوا على جواز بقائها بأنها لو كانت مستكنة الوجود فى الزمان الأول وتكون كذلك فى الزمان الثانى إذ لو جاز أن ينقلب الممكن لذاته في زمان ممتنعا في زمان آخر لجاز أن ينقلب الممتنع في زمان واجبا في زمان آخر ، وعلى هذا يجوز أن يكون العالم قبل وجوده ممتنعا الوجود لعينه ثم انقلب واجبا لعينه ، وعلى هذا التقدير يلزم نفى الصانع تعالى عنه علوا كبيرا . جو هو جزء من مفهوم السوادية أن السواد مما يتيقنه البصر واللون أحق بأن يكون صفة ، والجنس لا يكون عرضا قائما بالنوع ولا الجزء به يكل ، وأيضاً كون العرض حالا في محله إضافة لا وجود لها إلا في العقل كما ولا يتسلسل بل تقف عند وقوف العقل عن الاعتبار وكون الحلول نقيضاً للاحلول Examining the Causes of Annihilation
Razi explores the two types of causes for annihilation and rejects both.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari analysis of possible causes for an accident's annihilation and why each is problematic.

Core Idea
Every proposed cause for annihilating an enduring accident leads to contradiction.

Having established that the non-existence of an enduring accident would need a cause, Imam Razi now examines both existential and non-existential possibilities. He shows that an existential cause (whether necessitating or voluntary) leads to absurdities, and a non-existential cause (removal of a condition) fails because the condition (substance) remains. Thus, the very possibility of survival implies the impossibility of annihilation, which is absurd.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Follow the division into existential and non-existential causes.
2
Analyze the existential cause
Understand the objection against the opposite occurrence and the voluntary act of God.
3
Analyze the non-existential cause
Understand why removal of condition fails.
4
Grasp the conclusion
No valid cause exists, so survival is impossible.

As for the existential, it is either necessitating, as it is said that it annihilates by the occurrence of the opposite, but that is impossible because the occurrence of the opposite in a substratum is conditioned by the non-existence of the first opposite from it; if that non-existence were brought about by it, circularity would follow. Or it is voluntary, as it is said that God annihilates it, but that is impossible because the annihilator at the time of annihilation either issued an act or not; if an act issued, then it is an effect in producing an existential thing, so that would be creation, not annihilation; if no act issued, it is impossible because the powerful must have an effect. As for the non-existential cause, it is removed by the removal of its condition, but its condition is the substance which remains, and the discussion about how it becomes non-existent is like the discussion about how the accident becomes non-existent. So it is established that if its survival were valid, its non-existence would be impossible. Yet it may become non-existent, so survival is impossible. It was said: for the first, we do not grant that survival is an accident; if we grant it, why is it not permissible for such an accident to subsist in an accident? For the second, why is it not permissible for its non-existence to become necessary after its survival in a specific time? This is because the accident, according to you, was possible in the first time, then became impossible in the second time; so why is it not permissible for it to survive many times, then come to a time in which it becomes impossible in itself, and then it perishes without a cause? If we grant that it must have a cause, why is it not permissible for it to be removed by the removal of a condition, namely that the enduring accidents are conditioned by accidents that do not endure, so when they cease, the enduring perishes? There remains no way to refute this except induction, which yields only conjecture. Then they argued for the permissibility of their survival: if they were stable in existence in the first time and are also so in the second time, then if it is possible for the possible in itself to become impossible in another time, it would also be possible for the impossible to become necessary in another time. On this basis, it would be possible for the world before its existence to be impossible in itself, then to become necessary in itself, and on this estimation, the negation of the Creator would follow - exalted is He above that greatly. And is part of the concept of blackness that blackness is perceived by sight, and color is more deserving of being an attribute, and the genus is not an accident subsisting in the species nor the part in it, and also that an accident being in its substratum is a relation that has no existence except in the mind, and it does not regress infinitely but stops when the mind stops considering, and that inherence being the opposite of non-inherence does not necessitate the existence of inherence, as we have explained repeatedly, and referring the answer to what has passed is not useful here. Those who hold this say: every accident that inheres in a substratum imparts an attribute upon the substratum; speed makes motion fast and the body is not described by it, so it is an accident of motion, not of the body; and unity, if it is an accident, then the unity of the accident inheres in it; and the point is a division of the line, not of the body. I say: Abu al-Husayn al-Basri claims that knowledge of the survival of accidents like blackness and whiteness is necessary. His statement that the occurrence of the opposite in a substratum is conditioned by the non-existence of the first opposite is a mere claim, not accepted by those who say that the opposite is removed by the occurrence of its opposite; rather, they say the non-existence of the first opposite is caused by the occurrence of the opposite in its substratum, and preferring one of the two statements over the other requires evidence. Also his statement that the annihilator, if an act issues from him, his effect is in producing an existential thing is not granted by him, for he says his effect is a renewed thing, and that thing is not the creation of a non-existent but the annihilation of an existent. What is the evidence that the first is possible and not the second?
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage presents the Ash'ari position that accidents cannot endure for more than one moment. The argument is that if an accident could survive, it would require another accident (survival) to subsist in it, leading to accident-on-accident. Moreover, if survival were possible, then its non-existence would be impossible, leading to absurd consequences. The passage then examines possible causes for the non-existence of an enduring accident and refutes them. It also gives a proof from the philosophers that if accidents could be contingent at one time and impossible at another, then the world might have been impossible before creation, leading to atheism. The footnote by Razi criticizes Abu al-Husayn al-Basri's claim that knowledge of the survival of accidents is intuitive, and challenges his arguments about the conditions for the occurrence of opposites and about the nature of annihilation. The passage covers a wide range of arguments and responses.

Key Terms
طريان الضد
إيجاد
إعدام
شرط
جوهر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does Razi reject the idea that God annihilates an accident by a voluntary act?
A) Because God cannot will annihilation.
B) Because the act would be creation, not annihilation.
C) Because God has no effect.
D) Because accidents are eternal.

Key Takeaway
Every potential cause for the annihilation of an enduring accident fails, proving that survival would entail impossibility of annihilation, which is contradicted by observation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see change and decay around you—loved ones aging, objects breaking, feelings fading—do you assume a clear cause? Razi shows that every proposed cause for annihilation fails. Does this unsettle your need for explanations, or does it deepen your trust that God alone sustains every moment? Will you consciously pause before attributing loss to secondary causes, and instead recognize His direct control?
?
Razi argues that if an accident could endure, its annihilation would be impossible. Yet you live in a world of constant ending. How does this drive home the truth that nothing in creation—including your own health, reputation, or relationships—has any inherent permanence? What is one attachment you need to release today because it cannot truly last?
?
You say God is powerful. But Razi shows that even God cannot 'annihilate' by a direct act—because any act creates existence. How does this reshape your understanding of divine power? Are you willing to stop describing God's actions in human terms and instead submit to the fact that His ways are beyond your categories? What will it cost you to abandon that comfortable mental model?

Sects and Disagreements
advanced
6 min
Objections and the Philosophers' Argument
Razi presents objections and the philosophers' counter-argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objections raised against the Ash'ari proofs and the philosophers' argument against change in ontological status.

Core Idea
Opponents challenge the premises, and philosophers argue that if possibility status can change, the world could have been impossible before creation.

After presenting the Ash'ari proofs, Razi records objections to both the first and second arguments. The opponents question whether survival is an accident and whether non-existence can become necessary after survival. Then a further argument from the philosophers is presented: if an accident can change from possible to impossible over time, then the world itself could have been impossible, leading to atheism. This lesson captures those dialectical moves.

Guided Path
1
Read the objections
Notice how each proof is challenged.
2
Understand the philosophers' argument
See the reductio ad absurdum about possibility status.
3
Reflect on the implications
Why would changing possibility status lead to denial of the Creator?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : اتفقت الأشاعرة على امتناع بقاء العرض لأن البقاء صفة فلو بقى العرض لزم قيام العرض بالعرض ولأنه لو صح بقاء العرض لامتنع عدمه ، لأن عدمه بعد البقاء لا يجوز أن يكون واجباً وإلا لانقلب الشيء من الإمكان الذاتي إلى الامتناع ، بل يكون جائزا وله سبب وهو إما وجودى أو عدمى ، أما الوجود فإما الموجب كما يقال إنه يفنى الطريان الصد محال ، لأن طريان الضد على المحل مشروط بعدم الضد الأول عنه ، فلو عمل ذلك العدم به لزم الدور ، وإما المختار كما يقال الله تعالى بعدمه وهو محال لأن المعدم حين الإعدام إما أن يكون قد صدر عنه أمر أو لم يصدر ، فإن صدر عنه أمر فتأثير فى تحصيل أمر وجودى فهذا يكون إيجاداً لا إعداماً ، وإن لم يصدر عنه أمر فهو محال لأن القادر لا بد له من أثر ، وأما العدمي فإنه ينتفى لانتفاء شرطه لكن شرطه الجوهر وهو باق والكلام في كيفية عدمه كالكلام فى كيفية عدم العرض فثبت أنه لو صح بقاؤه لامتنع عدمه ، لكنه قد يعدم لامتنع بقاؤه فقيل على الأول لا نسلم أن البقاء عرض سلمناه لكن لم لا يجوز قيام مثل هذا العرض بالعرض ، وعلى الثانى لم لا يجوز أن يجب عدمه بعد بقائه في زمان معين ، وهذا لأن العرض عندكم كان جائز الوجود فى الزمان الأول ثم انقلب ممتنعا في الزمان الثانى فلم لا يجوز أن يبقى أزمنة كثيرة ثم ينتهي إلى زمان يصير فيه ممتنع الوجود بعينه وحينئذ يفنى لا لسبب سلمنا أنه لابد له من سبب لكن لم لا يجوز أن ينتفى لانتفاء الشرط وهو أن تكون الأعراض الباقية مشروطة بأعراض لا تبقى فعند انقطاعها يفنى الباقى ، ولا يبقى في دفع هذا الاحتمال إلا الاستقراء الذى لا يفيد إلا الظن، ثم احتجوا على جواز بقائها بأنها لو كانت مستكنة الوجود فى الزمان الأول وتكون كذلك فى الزمان الثانى إذ لو جاز أن ينقلب الممكن لذاته في زمان ممتنعا في زمان آخر لجاز أن ينقلب الممتنع في زمان واجبا في زمان آخر ، وعلى هذا يجوز أن يكون العالم قبل وجوده ممتنعا الوجود لعينه ثم انقلب واجبا لعينه ، وعلى هذا التقدير يلزم نفى الصانع تعالى عنه علوا كبيرا . جو هو جزء من مفهوم السوادية أن السواد مما يتيقنه البصر واللون أحق بأن يكون صفة ، والجنس لا يكون عرضا قائما بالنوع ولا الجزء به يكل ، وأيضاً كون العرض حالا في محله إضافة لا وجود لها إلا في العقل كما ولا يتسلسل بل تقف عند وقوف العقل عن الاعتبار وكون الحلول نقيضاً للاحلول لا يقتضى وجود الحلول كما بيناه مرارا وحوالة الجواب على ما مر غير مفيد ههنا. والقائلون به يقولون كل عرض يحل في محل فإنه يفيد صفة المحل، والسرعة تجعل الحركة سريعة ولا يوصف الجسم بها فهو عرض للحركة لا للجسم ، والوحدة إن كانت عرضا فوحدة العرض تحل فيه والنقطة فصل للخط لا للجسم . أقول : أبو الحسين البصرى يدعى أن العلم ببقاء الأعراض كالسواد والبياض ضرورى . وقوله : بأن طرو الضد على المحل مشروط بعدم الضد الأول دعوى مجردة لا يقبلها القائل بأن الضد ينتفى عند طريان ضده ، بل يقول عدم الضد الأول معلل بطريان الضد على محله وترجيح أحد القولين على الآخر محتاج إلى دليل . وقوله : المعدم إن صدر عنه أمر فتأثيره في تحصيل أمر وجودى أيضا غير مسلم عنده ، فإنه يقول تأثيره أمر متجدد وذلك الأمر ليس إيجاد معدوم بل هو إعدام موجود ، ما الدليل على أن الأول ممكن وحده دونه
Issue: The Ash'aris have agreed on the impossibility of the survival of an accident, because survival is an attribute; so if an accident survived, subsistence of an accident in an accident would follow. And because if the survival of an accident were valid, its non-existence would be impossible; for its non-existence after survival cannot be necessary, otherwise a thing would change from essential possibility to impossibility, rather it is possible and has a cause, which is either existential or non-existential. As for the existential, it is either necessitating, as it is said that it annihilates by the occurrence of the opposite, but that is impossible because the occurrence of the opposite in a substratum is conditioned by the non-existence of the first opposite from it; if that non-existence were brought about by it, circularity would follow. So it is established that if its survival were valid, its non-existence would be impossible. Yet it may become non-existent, so survival is impossible. Checkpoint
What is the consequence the philosophers draw from allowing possibility to change over time?
A) Accidents can endure.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that something is 'impossible' for you to change, when in reality your possibilities are entirely in Allah's hands? What does this passage remind you about relying on your own categories of possibility?
?
If you allowed that essential possibilities can change without cause, what would that imply about your dependence on Allah? Are there areas in your life where you act as if things are 'impossible' for Allah to change?
?
The philosophers argue that changing possibility leads to denying the Creator. Have you ever held a belief that seemed logical but, if fully examined, would undermine your tawheed? What will you do to root out such assumptions?

Razi's Critique of Abu al-Husayn al-Basri
Razi refutes Basri's claims and arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's refutation of Basri's claim that knowledge of accident survival is intuitive and his criticism of Basri's arguments.

Core Idea
Razi challenges Basri's claims that survival is self-evident and that his objections are mere assertions.

After presenting the philosophical arguments, Imam Razi turns his attention to Abu al-Husayn al-Basri, who argued that knowledge of accident survival (like blackness) is necessary and self-evident. Razi critiques Basri's premises, highlighting that Basri's objections to the Ash'ari arguments are not proven. This lesson shows Razi's critical engagement with opposing views.

Guided Path
1
Read Razi's opening
Understand the claim made by Basri.
2
Analyze the response to the opposite occurrence argument
Razi says Basri's condition is a mere claim.
3
Analyze the response to the annihilation argument
Razi questions Basri's view that the effect is not creation.

Issue: The Ash'aris have agreed on the impossibility of the survival of an accident, because survival is an attribute; so if an accident survived, subsistence of an accident in an accident would follow. And because if the survival of an accident were valid, its non-existence would be impossible; for its non-existence after survival cannot be necessary, otherwise a thing would change from essential possibility to impossibility, rather it is possible and has a cause, which is either existential or non-existential. As for the existential, it is either necessitating, as it is said that it annihilates by the occurrence of the opposite, but that is impossible because the occurrence of the opposite in a substratum is conditioned by the non-existence of the first opposite from it; if that non-existence were brought about by it, circularity would follow. Or it is voluntary, as it is said that God annihilates it, but that is impossible because the annihilator at the time of annihilation either issued an act or not; if an act issued, then it is an effect in producing an existential thing, so that would be creation, not annihilation; if no act issued, it is impossible because the powerful must have an effect. As for the non-existential cause, it is removed by the removal of its condition, but its condition is the substance which remains, and the discussion about how it becomes non-existent is like the discussion about how the accident becomes non-existent. So it is established that if its survival were valid, its non-existence would be impossible. Yet it may become non-existent, so survival is impossible. It was said: for the first, we do not grant that survival is an accident; if we grant it, why is it not permissible for such an accident to subsist in an accident? For the second, why is it not permissible for its non-existence to become necessary after its survival in a specific time? This is because the accident, according to you, was possible in the first time, then became impossible in the second time; so why is it not permissible for it to survive many times, then come to a time in which it becomes impossible in itself, and then it perishes without a cause? If we grant that it must have a cause, why is it not permissible for it to be removed by the removal of a condition, namely that the enduring accidents are conditioned by accidents that do not endure, so when they cease, the enduring perishes? There remains no way to refute this except induction, which yields only conjecture. Then they argued for the permissibility of their survival: if they were stable in existence in the first time and are also so in the second time, then if it is possible for the possible in itself to become impossible in another time, it would also be possible for the impossible to become necessary in another time. On this basis, it would be possible for the world before its existence to be impossible in itself, then to become necessary in itself, and on this estimation, the negation of the Creator would follow - exalted is He above that greatly. And is part of the concept of blackness that blackness is perceived by sight, and color is more deserving of being an attribute, and the genus is not an accident subsisting in the species nor the part in it, and also that an accident being in its substratum is a relation that has no existence except in the mind, and it does not regress infinitely but stops when the mind stops considering, and that inherence being the opposite of non-inherence does not necessitate the existence of inherence, as we have explained repeatedly, and referring the answer to what has passed is not useful here. Those who hold this say: every accident that inheres in a substratum imparts an attribute upon the substratum; speed makes motion fast and the body is not described by it, so it is an accident of motion, not of the body; and unity, if it is an accident, then the unity of the accident inheres in it; and the point is a division of the line, not of the body. I say: Abu al-Husayn al-Basri claims that knowledge of the survival of accidents like blackness and whiteness is necessary. His statement that the occurrence of the opposite in a substratum is conditioned by the non-existence of the first opposite is a mere claim, not accepted by those who say that the opposite is removed by the occurrence of its opposite; rather, they say the non-existence of the first opposite is caused by the occurrence of the opposite in its substratum, and preferring one of the two statements over the other requires evidence. Also his statement that the annihilator, if an act issues from him, his effect is in producing an existential thing is not granted by him, for he says his effect is a renewed thing, and that thing is not the creation of a non-existent but the annihilation of an existent. What is the evidence that the first is possible and not the second?
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage presents the Ash'ari position that accidents cannot endure for more than one moment. The argument is that if an accident could survive, it would require another accident (survival) to subsist in it, leading to accident-on-accident. Moreover, if survival were possible, then its non-existence would be impossible, leading to absurd consequences. The passage then examines possible causes for the non-existence of an enduring accident and refutes them. It also gives a proof from the philosophers that if accidents could be contingent at one time and impossible at another, then the world might have been impossible before creation, leading to atheism. The footnote by Razi criticizes Abu al-Husayn al-Basri's claim that knowledge of the survival of accidents is intuitive, and challenges his arguments about the conditions for the occurrence of opposites and about the nature of annihilation. The passage covers a wide range of arguments and responses.

Key Terms
بقاء
علم ضرورى
طرو الضد
إعدام
إيجاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does Razi say about Basri's claim that the annihilator's act is not creation but annihilation?
A) He agrees.
B) He asks for evidence that the first (creation) is possible and not the second.
C) He proves it is creation.
D) He ignores it.

Key Takeaway
Razi challenges Basri's claims, showing that his objections are not self-evident but require proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hold a strong opinion about a theological matter, do you ever treat it as 'self-evident' without seeking evidence? Which of your beliefs might actually be mere claims that you have not critically examined?
?
Razi demands proof for both sides of the debate. In your own life, are you quicker to demand evidence from others than from yourself? Where is that imbalance most harmful?
?
Imagine you heard a teacher dismiss a valid counterargument as 'just a claim' while assuming their own view is obvious. Are you ever that teacher in your own heart? How will you humble yourself to seek proof even for what seems obvious?

Four Opinions on Body Composition
Razi classifies four views on body composition using division criteria.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four classical opinions on the composition of bodies and how they are derived from two criteria.

Core Idea
Bodies are either divisible or not, finite or infinite — four views result.

Imam Razi presents a classification of the four opinions on body composition. The classification uses two binary criteria: actual versus potential division, and finite versus infinite. From these, four views emerge, covering theologians, philosophers, and a rejected position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage slowly. Notice how the criteria of actual/potential and finite/infinite generate four possibilities.
2
Map the criteria
Draw a 2x2 grid: rows = actual/potential, columns = finite/infinite. See which cell each opinion falls into.
3
Identify each opinion
Match the four descriptions to the labels: theologians, al-Nazzam, rejected view, philosophers.
4
Reflect on implications
Consider why the third view is rejected and how the first and fourth differ.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالانقسام الممكن إما أن يكون حاصلا بالفعل أو لا يكون كذلك، فعلى التقديرين فإما أن يكون متناهيا أو غير متناه، فخرج من هذا التقسيم أقسام أربعة. أحدها: أن الجسم مركب من أجزاء متناهية كل واحد منها لا يقبل القسمة أصلا وهو قول جمهور المتكلمين. وثانها: أنه مركب من أجزاء غير متناهية بالفعل وهو قول النظام. وثالثها: أنه غير مركب لكنه قابل لانقسامات متناهية وهو قول مردود. ورابعها: أنه غير مركب لكنه لا ينتهي في الصغر إلى حد إلا وهو بعد ذلك يكون قابلا للتقديم وهو قول جمهور الفلاسفة.
Possible division either is actual or not; in both cases, either finite or infinite. So from this division four classes emerge. First: that the body is composed of finite parts, each of which is not divisible at all—this is the statement of the majority of theologians. Second: that it is composed of actually infinite parts—this is the statement of al-Nazzam. Third: that it is not composed but is receptive to finite divisions—this is a rejected statement. Fourth: that it is not composed but does not reach a limit in smallness except that after that it is receptive to subdivision—this is the statement of the majority of philosophers.
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the four opinions regarding the composition of bodies. The classification is based on two criteria: whether division is actual or potential, and whether it is finite or infinite. From these criteria, four views are derived. The first view, held by the majority of theologians (mutakallimun), is that the body is composed of finite indivisible parts (atoms). The second view, attributed to al-Nazzam, is that the body is composed of actually infinite parts (i.e., no ultimate part). The third view is that the body is not composed but is capable of finite divisions—this is described as rejected. The fourth view, held by the majority of philosophers, is that the body is not composed but is infinitely divisible (i.e., no limit to smallness, always further divisible).

Key Terms
الانقسام
الجسم
الجزء
المتكلمون
النظام
الفلاسفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think only three views exist: atomism, infinite divisibility, and something else.
Correction: The passage explicitly lists four views; the third is rejected but necessary for logical completeness.

Checkpoint
According to the classification, which group holds that bodies are composed of finite indivisible parts?
A) The majority of theologians (mutakallimun)
B) Al-Nazzam
C) The majority of philosophers
D) Those who say the body is not composed but receptive to finite divisions

Key Takeaway
The classification of body composition depends on two criteria: actual/potential division and finitude, yielding four logical possibilities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you examine the logical grid of four views, which one did you instinctively favor? Is that instinct based on reason, upbringing, or a desire for control? How willing are you to let a logical classification challenge your default assumptions about the physical world?
?
The third view is rejected even though it completes the logical grid. What does this teach you about the relationship between logical possibility and truth? In your own life, are there beliefs you hold that are logically coherent but actually false? Will you commit to re-examining them?
?
Al-Nazzam's view (actually infinite parts) seems to stretch the imagination. Are you willing to accept a truth that feels intellectually uncomfortable if it comes from a reliable source? How does that apply to your acceptance of revelation over pure reason?
