Baseline Positions on Annihilation
The author enumerates three basic positions on annihilation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the foundational positions of the Karamiyya, al-Jāḥiẓ, the Ashʿarīs, and Abū Hāshim regarding the possibility of the world's annihilation.

Core Idea
The world's annihilation is debated: some deem it impossible, others possible, and some only known by revelation.

This lesson introduces the basic stances on whether the world can cease to exist. We begin with the Karamiyya and al-Jāḥiẓ, who argue annihilation is impossible, then the Ashʿarīs and Abū ʿAlī who affirm intellectual possibility, and finally Abū Hāshim who restricts knowledge to revelation. This sets the stage for the detailed mechanisms discussed in subsequent lessons.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation to grasp the three distinct positions.
Notice the author uses concise, parallel phrasing for each view.
2
Identify the three positions
List the positions: 1) impossibility (Karamiyya and al-Jāḥiẓ), 2) intellectual possibility (Ashʿarīs and Abū ʿAlī), 3) revealed knowledge only (Abū Hāshim).
Ask yourself: why does Abū Hāshim restrict knowledge to revelation?
3
Reflect on the key difference
Consider the difference between intellectual possibility and revealed knowledge. The former is a rational judgment, the latter a reliance on scripture.
This distinction is important for later debates on the role of reason in theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقول : : مذهب الكرامية أن العالم محدث ممتنع الفناء وإليه ذهب الجاحظ وقال الأشعرية وأبو على الجبائي يجوز فناء العالم عقلا ، وقال أبو هاشم إنما يعرف ذلك بالسمع ثم أن الاشعرية قالوا إنه يفنى من جهة أن الله تعالى لا يخلق الأعراض التى يحتاج الجواهر إلى وجودها ، أما القاضي أبو بكر قال في بعض المواضع أن تلك الأعراض هى الاكوان وقال فى بعض المواضع أن الفاعل المختار يفنى بلا واسطة و قال محمود الخياط وقال في موضع آخر أن الجوهر يحتاج إلى نوع من كل جنس من أجناس الأعراض ، فإذا لم يخلق أي نوع كان انعدام الجوهر وقال إمام الحرمين بمثل ذلك ، وقال بعضهم إذا لم يخلق البقاء وهو عرض انعدم الجوهر و به قال الكمي ، وقال أبو الهذيل كما أنه قال كن فكان يقول افن فيفنى، وقال أبو على وأبو هاشم إن الله يخلق الفناء وهى عرض فيفى جميع الأجسام وهو لا يبقى ، وأبو على يقول إنه يخلق لكل جوهر فناء والباقون قالوا بأن فناء واحداً يكفي لإفناء الكل فهذه مذاهبهم . وقول المصنف فى الإعدام : إنه باطل لأنه لا فرق بين أن يقال لم يفعل البتة وبين أن يقال جعل العدم ليس بشيء ، وذلك أن الفرق بينهما حاصل في بديهة المنظر ، فإن النول أنه لم يفعل حكم بالاستمرار على ما كان
I say: The school of the Karamiyya is that the world is temporally originated (muḥdath) and impossible to perish; al-Jāḥiẓ held this view. The Ashʿarīs and Abū ʿAlī al-Jubbāʾī said that the annihilation of the world is intellectually possible. Abū Hāshim said that it is known only through revelation. Then the Ashʿarīs said that it perishes in that God does not create the accidents that substances need for their existence. As for al-Qāḍī Abū Bakr, he said in some places that those accidents are the akwān (modes), and in other places he said that the free agent annihilates without an intermediary. Maḥmūd al-Khayyāṭ said in another place that the substance needs a type from every genus of accidents, so if no type is created, the substance becomes non-existent. Imām al-Ḥaramayn said something similar. Some said that if persistence (al-baqāʾ) is not created—and it is an accident—the substance becomes non-existent; al-Kaʿbī held this view. Abū al-Hudhayl said that just as He says 'Be' and it is, He says 'Perish' and it perishes. Abū ʿAlī and Abū Hāshim said that God creates annihilation (al-fanāʾ), which is an accident, and it annihilates all bodies, while it does not persist. Abū ʿAlī says He creates an annihilation for each substance, while others said that a single annihilation suffices for annihilating everything. These are their schools. And the author's statement concerning annihilation: it is invalid because there is no difference between saying 'he did not act at all' and saying 'he made non-existence'—it is not a thing. For the difference between them is clear to the intuitive intellect, because saying 'he did not act' is a judgment of continuation upon what was.
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage is a comprehensive survey by the author of the various Islamic theological schools' positions on the possibility and mechanism of the world's annihilation. He begins with the Karamiyya and al-Jāḥiẓ, who hold that the world, though temporally originated, cannot perish. The Ashʿarīs and Abū ʿAlī al-Jubbāʾī affirm intellectual possibility, while Abū Hāshim restricts knowledge of it to revelation. The author then details the Ashʿarīs' mechanism: God refrains from creating the accidents on which substances depend, causing the substances to cease. He notes differences among Ashʿarī scholars: al-Qāḍī Abū Bakr sometimes identifies those accidents as modes (akwān) and sometimes says God annihilates directly; Maḥmūd al-Khayyāṭ requires a type from each genus of accidents; Imām al-Ḥaramayn agrees; some hold persistence (baqāʾ) as a necessary accident; Abū al-Hudhayl posits a direct command 'Perish'; Abū ʿAlī and Abū Hāshim introduce a created accident of annihilation (fanāʾ) that annihilates all bodies, with disagreement on whether each substance requires its own annihilation or a single one suffices. The passage ends with a critical note: the author rejects the idea that annihilation is mere non-action, asserting that the difference between non-action and causing non-existence is intuitively obvious.

Key Terms
فناء
محدث
عقلا
سمع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All Islamic scholars agree the world will be annihilated.
Correction: The Karamiyya and al-Jāḥiẓ hold it is impossible; others differ.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, who said the world is impossible to perish?
A) Karamiyya and al-Jāḥiẓ
B) Ashʿarīs
C) Abū Hāshim
D) All of them

Key Takeaway
The three foundational positions set the stage for detailed theological debate on the mechanism of annihilation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If you held the Karamiyya view that the world cannot perish, would you feel less urgency about preparing for the Hereafter? Why or why not?
?
How does knowing that some scholars limit knowledge of annihilation to revelation affect your own approach to studying theological issues? Are you comfortable relying on revelation where reason is silent?

Ash'ari Mechanisms of Annihilation
The author presents various Ash'ari explanations for the annihilation of substances.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the variations within Ash'ari theology on how the world perishes, including the roles of accidents, persistence, and the free agent.

Core Idea
Ash'aris explain annihilation as God's withholding of accidents, with differing accounts of which accidents and how.

This lesson dives into the mechanisms proposed by Ash'ari theologians to explain how the world, though created, can cease to exist. We explore the views of al-Qāḍī Abū Bakr, Maḥmūd al-Khayyāṭ, and Imām al-Ḥaramayn, who link annihilation to the non-creation of essential accidents. Then we encounter the theory that persistence (baqā') itself is an accident whose absence causes annihilation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Note the different opinions within the Ash'ari school.
The passage lists multiple views, sometimes attributed to the same scholar (e.g., al-Qāḍī Abū Bakr has two views).
2
Identify the key mechanisms
List the mechanisms: non-creation of accidents (general), non-creation of akwān, direct annihilation without intermediary, need for a type from every genus, non-creation of baqā'.
Notice that each mechanism links the substance's existence to specific accidents.
3
Compare the role of the free agent
Consider how each view describes God's action: some by withholding, some by direct act, some by creating a specific accident of persistence.
The variety shows different ways to conceive of God's causal power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقول : : مذهب الكرامية أن العالم محدث ممتنع الفناء وإليه ذهب الجاحظ وقال الأشعرية وأبو على الجبائي يجوز فناء العالم عقلا ، وقال أبو هاشم إنما يعرف ذلك بالسمع ثم أن الاشعرية قالوا إنه يفنى من جهة أن الله تعالى لا يخلق الأعراض التى يحتاج الجواهر إلى وجودها ، أما القاضي أبو بكر قال في بعض المواضع أن تلك الأعراض هى الاكوان وقال فى بعض المواضع أن الفاعل المختار يفنى بلا واسطة و قال محمود الخياط وقال في موضع آخر أن الجوهر يحتاج إلى نوع من كل جنس من أجناس الأعراض ، فإذا لم يخلق أي نوع كان انعدام الجوهر وقال إمام الحرمين بمثل ذلك ، وقال بعضهم إذا لم يخلق البقاء وهو عرض انعدم الجوهر و به قال الكمي ، وقال أبو الهذيل كما أنه قال كن فكان يقول افن فيفنى، وقال أبو على وأبو هاشم إن الله يخلق الفناء وهى عرض فيفى جميع الأجسام وهو لا يبقى ، وأبو على يقول إنه يخلق لكل جوهر فناء والباقون قالوا بأن فناء واحداً يكفي لإفناء الكل فهذه مذاهبهم . وقول المصنف فى الإعدام : إنه باطل لأنه لا فرق بين أن يقال لم يفعل البتة وبين أن يقال جعل العدم ليس بشيء ، وذلك أن الفرق بينهما حاصل في بديهة المنظر ، فإن النول أنه لم يفعل حكم بالاستمرار على ما كان
I say: The school of the Karamiyya is that the world is temporally originated (muḥdath) and impossible to perish; al-Jāḥiẓ held this view. The Ashʿarīs and Abū ʿAlī al-Jubbāʾī said that the annihilation of the world is intellectually possible. Abū Hāshim said that it is known only through revelation. Then the Ashʿarīs said that it perishes in that God does not create the accidents that substances need for their existence. As for al-Qāḍī Abū Bakr, he said in some places that those accidents are the akwān (modes), and in other places he said that the free agent annihilates without an intermediary. Maḥmūd al-Khayyāṭ said in another place that the substance needs a type from every genus of accidents, so if no type is created, the substance becomes non-existent. Imām al-Ḥaramayn said something similar. Some said that if persistence (al-baqāʾ) is not created—and it is an accident—the substance becomes non-existent; al-Kaʿbī held this view. Abū al-Hudhayl said that just as He says 'Be' and it is, He says 'Perish' and it perishes. Abū ʿAlī and Abū Hāshim said that God creates annihilation (al-fanāʾ), which is an accident, and it annihilates all bodies, while it does not persist. Abū ʿAlī says He creates an annihilation for each substance, while others said that a single annihilation suffices for annihilating everything. These are their schools. And the author's statement concerning annihilation: it is invalid because there is no difference between saying 'he did not act at all' and saying 'he made non-existence'—it is not a thing. For the difference between them is clear to the intuitive intellect, because saying 'he did not act' is a judgment of continuation upon what was.
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage is a comprehensive survey by the author of the various Islamic theological schools' positions on the possibility and mechanism of the world's annihilation. He begins with the Karamiyya and al-Jāḥiẓ, who hold that the world, though temporally originated, cannot perish. The Ashʿarīs and Abū ʿAlī al-Jubbāʾī affirm intellectual possibility, while Abū Hāshim restricts knowledge of it to revelation. The author then details the Ashʿarīs' mechanism: God refrains from creating the accidents on which substances depend, causing the substances to cease. He notes differences among Ashʿarī scholars: al-Qāḍī Abū Bakr sometimes identifies those accidents as modes (akwān) and sometimes says God annihilates directly; Maḥmūd al-Khayyāṭ requires a type from each genus of accidents; Imām al-Ḥaramayn agrees; some hold persistence (baqāʾ) as a necessary accident; Abū al-Hudhayl posits a direct command 'Perish'; Abū ʿAlī and Abū Hāshim introduce a created accident of annihilation (fanāʾ) that annihilates all bodies, with disagreement on whether each substance requires its own annihilation or a single one suffices. The passage ends with a critical note: the author rejects the idea that annihilation is mere non-action, asserting that the difference between non-action and causing non-existence is intuitively obvious.

Misconception & Correction

Correction: The passage shows multiple mechanisms even within Ash'arism.

Checkpoint
According to Maḥmūd al-Khayyāṭ, what does the substance need for existence?
A) A specific accident
B) A type from every genus of accidents
C) The accident of persistence
D) Direct annihilation by God

Key Takeaway
Ash'ari explanations for annihilation center on God's withholding of accidents necessary for substance existence, with variations on which accidents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just read that, according to some Ashʿarīs, your very existence is sustained by God's continuous creation of accidents. If He were to stop creating even a single category of accidents, you would cease to be. Does your daily life reflect this absolute dependence, or do you live as if your existence is self-guaranteed? What one habit or assumption would change if you truly felt this dependence every moment?
?
Which of the mechanisms presented in this passage—whether direct annihilation, withholding of specific accidents, or withholding of persistence—resonates most deeply with your understanding of your own fragility before God? How might that mechanism reshape your view of your own mortality and your need for God's constant mercy?
?
The passage records multiple scholarly disagreements on how annihilation occurs. If the precise 'how' of your own nonexistence is beyond your knowledge and even the scholars differed, does that humbling uncertainty draw you closer to trusting God's absolute wisdom, or does it unsettle you? What would it mean for you to place your ultimate security not in understanding the mechanism but in the One who holds all mechanisms in His hand?

Direct Command and the Fanā' Accident
The author presents final views and adds his critique.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the views of Abū al-Hudhayl, Abū ʿAlī, and Abū Hāshim on annihilation, as well as the author's critique of the non-action theory.

Core Idea
Annihilation is explained either as a direct divine command or as a created accident, and the author argues it is distinct from mere non-action.

This final lesson covers the remaining positions: Abū al-Hudhayl's analogy to the command 'Be', and the view of Abū ʿAlī and Abū Hāshim that God creates an accident of annihilation (fanā'). The author then critiques a rival theory that equates annihilation with non-action, insisting that the intuitive mind distinguishes between not acting and causing non-existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Focus on the two positive views and the author's critique.
The critique is directed at a view not explicitly named here (the 'author's statement').
2
Distinguish the two mechanisms
Compare Abū al-Hudhayl's command model with the fanā' accident model of Abū ʿAlī and Abū Hāshim.
Note the subsidiary disagreement: one fanā' for all vs. one fanā' per substance.
3
Understand the author's critique
The author rejects equating annihilation with non-action. He argues that the intuitive intellect sees a clear difference between 'not doing' and 'making non-existent'.
This critique highlights the importance of causal language in theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

?
When you face trials or loss, do you see God’s hand actively bringing about change, or do you attribute it to mere 'circumstances'? How can you train yourself to recognize His active will in both creation and removal?

The Possibility of Annihilating the World: Early Theological Positions
The editor lists three positions on annihilation's possibility from different schools.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three main positions on whether the world can be annihilated: impossibility, intellectual possibility, and reliance on revelation alone.

Core Idea
Theologians disagreed: is annihilation possible or known only through revelation?

The debate about the world's annihilation touches on God's power and the nature of creation. Some schools, like the Karramiyya, argued that the world, once created, cannot cease to exist. Others, including the Ash'aris, maintained that annihilation is intellectually possible. A third view, held by Abu Hashim, insisted that such knowledge comes only from revelation. This lesson introduces these foundational positions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three positions
Read the passage carefully and identify each of the three positions mentioned: (1) Karramiyya and al-Jahiz, (2) Ash'aris and Abu Ali al-Jubbai, (3) Abu Hashim. Notice how each is introduced with 'قال' or 'ذهب'.
The positions are presented without argument, as a summary.
2
Compare the scope of each view
Consider what each position claims about reason vs. revelation. The first two are based on reason (one denying, one affirming possibility), the third defers to revelation. Ask yourself: why might someone insist on revelation alone?
This touches on broader epistemological issues in kalam.
3
Reflect on the significance
Think about why this disagreement matters. The annihilation of the world relates to God's power, the nature of created things, and eschatology. The positions shape how each school interprets related questions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقول: مذهب الكرامية أن العالم محدث ممتنع الفناء وإليه ذهب الجاحظ وقال الأشعرية وأبو على الجبائي يجوز فناء العالم عقلا ، وقال أبو هاشم إنما يعرف ذلك بالسمع ثم أن الأشعرية قالوا إنه يفنى من جهة أن الله تعالى لا يخلق الأعراض التي يحتاج الجواهر إلى وجودها ، أما القاضي أبو بكر قال في بعض المواضع أن تلك الأعراض هي الأكوان وقال في بعض المواضع أن الفاعل المختار يفنى بلا واسطة و قال محمود الخياط وقال في موضع آخر أن الجوهر يحتاج إلى نوع من كل جنس من أجناس الأعراض ، فإذا لم يخلق أي نوع كان انعدام الجوهر وقال إمام الحرمين بمثل ذلك ، وقال بعضهم إذا لم يخلق البقاء وهو عرض انعدم الجوهر و به قال الكمي ، وقال أبو الهذيل كما أنه قال كن فكان يقول افن فيفنى، وقال أبو علي وأبو هاشم إن الله يخلق الفناء وهي عرض فيفنى جميع الأجسام وهو لا يبقى ، وأبو علي يقول إنه يخلق لكل جوهر فناء والباقون قالوا بأن فناء واحدا يكفي لإفناء الكل فهذه مذاهبهم . وقول المصنف في الإعدام : إنه باطل لأنه لا فرق بين أن يقال لم يفعل البتة وبين أن يقال جعل العدم ليس بشيء ، وذلك أن الفرق بينهما حاصل في بديهة الناظر ، فإن القول أنه لم يفعل حكم بالاستمرار على ما كان و عدم صدور شيء عن الفاعل ، والقول أنه فعل العدم حكم بتجدد العدم بعد أن لم يكن وصدوره عن فاعل و تمايز العدمين يكون بانتسابهما إلى وجودين أو بانتساب أحدهما دون الآخر .
I say: The doctrine of the Karramiyya is that the world is originated (muḥdath) and impossible of annihilation; al-Jāḥiẓ held this. The Ash‘arīs and Abū ‘Alī al-Jubbā’ī said that the annihilation of the world is intellectually possible. Abū Hāshim said that this is only known through revelation. Then the Ash‘arīs said that it is annihilated by way of God not creating the accidents which substances need for their existence. As for Qāḍī Abū Bakr, he said in some places that those accidents are the akwān (modes), and in some places that the free agent annihilates without intermediary. Maḥmūd al-Khiyāṭ said in another place that the substance needs a type from every genus of accidents, so if any type is not created, the substance ceases. Imām al-Ḥaramayn said something similar. Some said that if God does not create 'remaining' (al-baqā') which is an accident, the substance ceases; this is held by al-Kummī. Abū al-Hudhayl said: just as He said 'Be' and it was, He says 'Perish' and it perishes. Abū ‘Alī and Abū Hāshim said that God creates annihilation (al-fanā'), which is an accident, and it annihilates all bodies while itself not remaining. Abū ‘Alī says that He creates for every substance an annihilation, and the rest said that one annihilation suffices for the annihilation of all. These are their doctrines. And the author's statement regarding annihilation: It is false because there is no difference between saying 'He did nothing at all' and saying 'He made non-existence' as a thing? Actually, the difference between them is evident to the reflective mind: the statement that He did nothing is a judgment of continuation on what was and of no effect issuing from the agent; the statement that He acted on non-existence is a judgment of the renewal of non-existence after it was not, and its issuing from an agent; and the distinction between the two non-existences is by their relation to two existences or by the relation of one of them without the other.
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage is an editorial summary of various Islamic theological positions on how the world can be annihilated. It is not part of al-Razi's main argument but a supplementary note. The editor first lists the Karramiyya and al-Jahiz who deny annihilation. Then the Ash'aris and Mu'tazilites who affirm its possibility. Different Ash'aris propose different mechanisms: (1) God ceases to create the accidents that substances need (the common view). (2) Qadi Abu Bakr: the free agent annihilates directly or via ceasing the 'akwan' (modes). (3) Mahmud al-Khiyat and Imam al-Haramayn: substances need a type from each genus of accidents. (4) Some: if God does not create the accident of permanence (baqa'), the substance ceases. (5) Abu al-Hudhayl: God says 'Perish' and it perishes. (6) Abu Ali and Abu Hashim: God creates an accident of annihilation (fana') which annihilates all bodies but does not itself remain (they differ on whether each substance gets its own annihilation). The editor then notes that al-Razi considers the objection that annihilation is false because there is no difference between non-action and making non-existence, and argues that the difference is evident.

Key Terms
الفناء
الكرامية
الجاحظ
الأشعرية
أبو علي الجبائي
أبو هاشم
السمع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All theologians agree that the world can be annihilated.
Correction: The passage shows that some deny annihilation altogether, and others say it is only known from revelation.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which school said that annihilation is impossible?
A) The Ash'aris
B) The Karramiyya
C) Abu Hashim
D) The Mu'tazilites

Key Takeaway
Early theological schools held three distinct views on annihilation: impossible, possible by reason, or known only by revelation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you treat the temporary nature of this world as a mere intellectual doctrine, or does it shape how you invest your heart and time? What would change if you lived as though the world could end at any moment?
?
The Karramiyya denied annihilation; the Ash'aris affirmed it by reason; Abu Hashim relied solely on revelation. Whose approach to certainty about the afterlife most reflects your own spiritual state, and why?
?
If you were to die tonight, would the theological debates over annihilation matter at all to your soul's salvation? What does your answer reveal about your attachment to argument versus practical faith?

How Does Annihilation Occur? Diverse Ash‘arī Theories
The editor enumerates various Ash'ari and Mu'tazilite views on how annihilation occurs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the various mechanisms proposed by Ash'ari theologians for how God annihilates the world.

Core Idea
Ash‘arīs proposed multiple mechanisms, from ceasing accidents to creating an accident of annihilation.

While Ash'aris agreed that annihilation is possible, they debated the mechanism. Some said God simply stops creating the accidents that sustain substances. Others proposed that the free agent annihilates directly, or that substance needs a type from every genus of accidents. This lesson surveys the main theories.

Guided Path
1
Read the list of mechanisms
Read the passage from 'ثم أن الأشعرية قالوا' to 'فهذه مذاهبهم'. Notice that each new mechanism is introduced with 'وقال' or 'و قال'.
This is a summary, so each view is stated concisely.
2
Categorize the mechanisms
Group the views: (a) ceasing accidents (common Ash'ari), (b) direct annihilation by agent, (c) need for a type from each genus, (d) need for the accident of 'baqa'', (e) command 'Perish', (f) creation of an accident of annihilation (with dispute on one vs. many).
Some views are attributed to specific scholars.
3
Identify disputes within schools
Notice that even within the Ash'ari school, there is disagreement. Qadi Abu Bakr is reported to have two different statements. Also note the difference between Abu Ali and the majority on multiple annihilations.
4
Reflect on the common thread
Despite differences, all mechanisms assume God's direct or indirect causal role in annihilation. This reflects the Ash'ari occasionalist worldview where God is the sole cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Different Ash'aris propose different mechanisms: (1) God ceases to create the accidents that substances need (the common view). (2) Qadi Abu Bakr: the free agent annihilates directly or via ceasing the 'akwan' (modes). (3) Mahmud al-Khiyat and Imam al-Haramayn: substances need a type from each genus of accidents. (4) Some: if God does not create the accident of permanence (baqa'), the substance ceases. (5) Abu al-Hudhayl: God says 'Perish' and it perishes. (6) Abu Ali and Abu Hashim: God creates an accident of annihilation (fana') which annihilates all bodies but does not itself remain (they differ on whether each substance gets its own annihilation). The editor then notes that al-Razi considers the objection that annihilation is false because there is no difference between non-action and making non-existence, and argues that the difference is evident.

الفاعل المختار
أبو القاسم الخياط
إمام الحرمين
أبو الهذيل
الكمي

Checkpoint

B) God says 'Perish' and it perishes
C) God creates an accident of annihilation
D) God removes the accident of remaining

Key Takeaway
Ash'ari theologians proposed at least six distinct mechanisms for annihilation, reflecting internal debate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson shows that great scholars disagreed on how annihilation occurs. Reflect: When you encounter a theological difference, do you tend to dismiss unfamiliar views or explore them as these scholars did? Commit to studying one view you disagree with this week with the intention of understanding, not refuting.
?
The Ash‘arīs offered multiple explanations for the same divine act. Is there an area of your faith where you demand a single explanation, rather than accepting valid diversity? Pray for humility to acknowledge that God's actions may surpass your understanding.
?
Several views propose that annihilation happens by God not doing something (ceasing to create accidents). How does this challenge your assumption that God is always actively doing? In your spiritual life, do you recognize moments when God's 'not doing' is also a form of guidance? Journal about one such moment today.

Al-Razi’s Rebuttal: Is Making Non-Existence Different from Inaction?
Al-Razi rebuts an objection that making non-existence is indistinguishable from inaction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument that there is a real distinction between 'not acting' and 'acting to make something non-existent'.

Core Idea
Making non-existence is a positive act, not merely inaction.

Some object that 'making non-existence' is meaningless because it is equivalent to doing nothing. The author (al-Razi) argues that the reflective mind distinguishes between the two: one implies continuation of non-action, the other implies a new event of non-existence brought about by an agent. This lesson unpacks that distinction.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the first part of the passage: 'وقول المصنف في الإعدام : إنه باطل لأنه لا فرق بين أن يقال لم يفعل البتة وبين أن يقال جعل العدم ليس بشيء'. The objection claims that making non-existence is no different from doing nothing at all.
The objector equates 'making non-existence' with inaction because non-existence is not a thing.
2
Follow al-Razi's response
Al-Razi asserts that the difference is obvious to the reflective mind. He contrasts: 'he did nothing' implies continuation of a prior state and no effect from the agent; 'he made non-existence' implies a new non-existence that issues from an agent.
The key is that the act of making non-existence introduces a change attributable to the agent.
3
Distinguish the two non-existences
Al-Razi adds that two non-existences can be distinguished by their relation to existences: either each relates to a different prior existence, or one does and the other does not. This shows non-existence can be a 'made' thing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقول: مذهب الكرامية أن العالم محدث ممتنع الفناء وإليه ذهب الجاحظ وقال الأشعرية وأبو على الجبائي يجوز فناء العالم عقلا ، وقال أبو هاشم إنما يعرف ذلك بالسمع ثم أن الأشعرية قالوا إنه يفنى من جهة أن الله تعالى لا يخلق الأعراض التي يحتاج الجواهر إلى وجودها ، أما القاضي أبو بكر قال في بعض المواضع أن تلك الأعراض هي الأكوان وقال في بعض المواضع أن الفاعل المختار يفنى بلا واسطة و قال محمود الخياط وقال في موضع آخر أن الجوهر يحتاج إلى نوع من كل جنس من أجناس الأعراض ، فإذا لم يخلق أي نوع كان انعدام الجوهر وقال إمام الحرمين بمثل ذلك ، وقال بعضهم إذا لم يخلق البقاء وهو عرض انعدم الجوهر و به قال الكمي ، وقال أبو الهذيل كما أنه قال كن فكان يقول افن فيفنى، وقال أبو علي وأبو هاشم إن الله يخلق الفناء وهي عرض فيفنى جميع الأجسام وهو لا يبقى ، وأبو علي يقول إنه يخلق لكل جوهر فناء والباقون قالوا بأن فناء واحدا يكفي لإفناء الكل فهذه مذاهبهم . وقول المصنف في الإعدام : إنه باطل لأنه لا فرق بين أن يقال لم يفعل البتة وبين أن يقال جعل العدم ليس بشيء ، وذلك أن الفرق بينهما حاصل في بديهة الناظر ، فإن القول أنه لم يفعل حكم بالاستمرار على ما كان و عدم صدور شيء عن الفاعل ، والقول أنه فعل العدم حكم بتجدد العدم بعد أن لم يكن وصدوره عن فاعل و تمايز العدمين يكون بانتسابهما إلى وجودين أو بانتساب أحدهما دون الآخر .
I say: The doctrine of the Karramiyya is that the world is originated (muḥdath) and impossible of annihilation; al-Jāḥiẓ held this. The Ash‘arīs and Abū ‘Alī al-Jubbā’ī said that the annihilation of the world is intellectually possible. Abū Hāshim said that this is only known through revelation. Then the Ash‘arīs said that it is annihilated by way of God not creating the accidents which substances need for their existence. As for Qāḍī Abū Bakr, he said in some places that those accidents are the akwān (modes), and in some places that the free agent annihilates without intermediary. Maḥmūd al-Khiyāṭ said in another place that the substance needs a type from every genus of accidents, so if any type is not created, the substance ceases. Imām al-Ḥaramayn said something similar. Some said that if God does not create 'remaining' (al-baqā') which is an accident, the substance ceases; this is held by al-Kummī. Abū al-Hudhayl said: just as He said 'Be' and it was, He says 'Perish' and it perishes. Abū ‘Alī and Abū Hāshim said that God creates annihilation (al-fanā'), which is an accident, and it annihilates all bodies while itself not remaining. Abū ‘Alī says that He creates for every substance an annihilation, and the rest said that one annihilation suffices for the annihilation of all. These are their doctrines. And the author's statement regarding annihilation: It is false because there is no difference between saying 'He did nothing at all' and saying 'He made non-existence' as a thing? Actually, the difference between them is evident to the reflective mind: the statement that He did nothing is a judgment of continuation on what was and of no effect issuing from the agent; the statement that He acted on non-existence is a judgment of the renewal of non-existence after it was not, and its issuing from an agent; and the distinction between the two non-existences is by their relation to two existences or by the relation of one of them without the other.
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage is an editorial summary of various Islamic theological positions on how the world can be annihilated. It is not part of al-Razi's main argument but a supplementary note. The editor first lists the Karramiyya and al-Jahiz who deny annihilation. Then the Ash'aris and Mu'tazilites who affirm its possibility. Different Ash'aris propose different mechanisms: (1) God ceases to create the accidents that substances need (the common view). (2) Qadi Abu Bakr: the free agent annihilates directly or via ceasing the 'akwan' (modes). (3) Mahmud al-Khiyat and Imam al-Haramayn: substances need a type from each genus of accidents. (4) Some: if God does not create the accident of permanence (baqa'), the substance ceases. (5) Abu al-Hudhayl: God says 'Perish' and it perishes. (6) Abu Ali and Abu Hashim: God creates an accident of annihilation (fana') which annihilates all bodies but does not itself remain (they differ on whether each substance gets its own annihilation). The editor then notes that al-Razi considers the objection that annihilation is false because there is no difference between non-action and making non-existence, and argues that the difference is evident.

Key Terms
الإعدام
العدم
الفعل
الفاعل
التجدد
الانتساب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Making non-existence is meaningless because non-existence is nothing.
Correction: Al-Razi argues that 'making non-existence' is a distinct act that brings about a new non-existence, different from simply not acting.

Checkpoint
How does al-Razi distinguish between 'he did nothing' and 'he made non-existence'?
A) Both are the same in meaning
B) 'He did nothing' implies continuation; 'he made non-existence' implies a new non-existence from an agent
C) 'He did nothing' implies a new action; 'he made non-existence' implies no action

Key Takeaway
Making non-existence is a genuine act distinct from inaction, as it introduces a new relational property attributable to an agent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Razi shows that 'doing nothing' is merely continuing a previous state, while 'making non-existence' is a positive act. When was the last time you excused your omission as 'just not doing anything' when it was actually an active failure to act? Will you confess that to Allah today as a sin of commission, not just omission?
?
If two non-existences can be distinguished by their relation to different existences, then your failures to act are not just empty blanks; they are unique acts tied to specific opportunities. What specific opportunity have you annihilated by your inaction that you cannot reclaim, and how will you be accountable for it?

Critique of the Classification of Spiritual Substances
Marginal note critiquing attribution of non-spatial substances to philosophers only.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the critique against attributing the affirmation of non-spatial substances exclusively to philosophers, and recognize the contributions of Mu'tazila scholars like Abu al-Qasim al-Balkhi.

Core Idea
Immaterial substances were affirmed by Mu'tazila too, not just philosophers.

The marginal note challenges the main text's assertion that only philosophers affirm substances that are neither spatially distinct nor inhering in bodies. It highlights that Mu'tazili scholar Abu al-Qasim al-Balkhi also held such a view. Additionally, it questions the inclusion of hyle among spiritual substances and notes that celestial souls and intellects were merely mentioned, not proven. This lesson unpacks these critical points.

Guided Path
1
Read the marginal note and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the critical tone and the specific points raised.
The note uses technical kalam terms; refer to the key terms list if needed.
2
Identify the main critique
Identify the central claim being critiqued: that only philosophers affirm substances without spatial distinction or inherence. The note calls this into question.
3
Analyze the counterexample: al-Balkhi
Examine the evidence provided: Abu al-Qasim al-Balkhi, a Mu'tazili, affirmed the human spirit as a substance without the attribute of distinction (صفة الميز). This contradicts the exclusivity claim.
Al-Balkhi was a leading Mu'tazili theologian; his view shows intra-school diversity.
4
Understand the objection regarding hyle
Consider why the inclusion of hyle (prime matter) among spiritual substances is problematic. The note says it is 'not satisfactory' to those who affirm spiritual substances.
Hyle is typically considered material; its classification as spiritual is contested.
5
Reflect on the treatment of celestial souls and intellects
Note that the author only mentioned celestial souls and intellects by name without affirming them. This omission weakens the classification schema.
The reference to 'حكايات الجرمانية' is obscure; it may refer to anecdotes or specific doctrines.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقول : بأن القائلين بالجواهر التي لا تكون متميزة ولا حالة فيه هم الفلاسفة فيه نظر لأن أبا القاسم البلخي من المعتزلة وأتباعه ذكروا أن الروح الإنساني جوهر ليس له صفة الميز وإيراد الهيولى ههنا على أنها من الجواهر الروحانية ليس بمرضى عند القائلين بها وأما النفوس السماوية والعقول فلم يتكلم عليها في هذا الكتاب مما يدل على إثباتها إنما أورد حكايات الجرمانية فيها فقط وذكرها عند قسمة الجوهر على رأي الفلاسفة بأسمائها فقط.
I say: that the proponents of substances that are neither distinguished nor inhering are the philosophers is questionable, because Abu al-Qasim al-Balkhi of the Mu'tazila and his followers mentioned that the human spirit is a substance that does not have the attribute of distinction. And the inclusion of hyle here as among the spiritual substances is not satisfactory to those who affirm them. As for celestial souls and intellects, he did not discuss them in this book in a way that indicates their affirmation; he only mentioned some [؟] anecdotes about them and mentioned them in the division of substance according to the philosophers' view only by their names.
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This marginal note (by the commentator) critiques the main text's claim that only philosophers affirm non-spatial substances. He points out that Abu al-Qasim al-Balkhi (a Mu'tazili) and his followers also held that the human spirit is a substance without spatial distinction. Furthermore, he objects to including hyle among spiritual substances, as this is not accepted by those who affirm such substances. He also notes that the author did not actually prove celestial souls and intellects in this book; he merely mentioned them by name in the division of substance according to philosophers. Thus, the main text’s classification is imprecise.

Impossibility of Combining Two Similars: Argument and Rebuttal
Razi argues against combining two similars with objection and critique.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument for the impossibility of combining two similars, the Mu'tazila objection, and the commentator's critical response.

Core Idea
Combining two similars collapses them into one, but lack of distinction does not prove identity.

In this passage, Razi defends the Ash'ari and philosophical stance that two similars cannot coexist, against the Mu'tazila who allow it. He presents a concise proof, then answers an objection, and finally a commentator adds critical nuances and a counterexample. This lesson walks through each part of the debate.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to get an overview of the argument structure.
Notice the flow: thesis → proof → objection → answer → commentator's critique.
2
Analyze the proof against combination
Identify the key premise: if two similars combine, no distinction remains, so they become one. Break down why distinction cannot be by essence or accident.
The proof assumes that without distinction, numerical identity follows.
3
Examine the objection and initial response
The opponent argues using 'ruling of a thing applies to its similar'. Understand how the response simply asserts that combination destroys duality.
The commentator later criticizes this response as a bare claim.
4
Understand the commentator's critique
The commentator refutes the idea that lack of distinction implies union, using the example of convergent lines. He also notes logical issues in classifying similars as opposites.
This critique undermines the original proof's inference.
5
Synthesize the positions
Reflect on the three perspectives: Ash'ari/philosophers (impossible), Mu'tazila (possible), and the commentator's nuanced view that the proof is insufficient.
The lesson's core idea becomes clear: argument over sameness versus distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : يستحيل الجمع بين المثلين عندنا وعند الفلاسفة خلافا للمعتزلة لنا أن بتقدير الاجتماع لا يحصل الامتياز بالذاتيات واللوازم وإلا لما هنا مثلين ، ولا بالعرض لأن نسبة ض إلى كل واحد منهما على السوية فلا يكون كونه عارضا لأحدهما أولى من كونه عارضا الآخر جميع العوارض ! فيكون علة لكل واحد منهما وحينئذ لا يبقى الامتياز بينهما البتة فيكون الاثنان واحدا وهو محال . احتج الخصم بأن حكم الشيء حكم مثله فإذا كانت الذات قابلة للآخر . جوابه أن الاجتماع يوجب انقلاب الاثنين واحدا (٢) أقول : عدم الامتياز لا يدل على الاتحاد بل غايته أن يدل على عدم العلم بالنفاير ، والحكم بأن المثلين المجتمعين لا يتمايزان بالعوارض منقوض بانحراف الخطوط المجتمعة التي تصير عند الاجتماع نقطة واحدة في الوضع فإنها انحراف خطوط متغايرة وكونها كذلك من عوارضها ، والحكم بأن الاجتماع يوجب انقلاب الاثنين واحدا دعوى مجردة عن بيان ، ومشايع المعتزلة جوزوا جمع المثلين وقالوا العملة في كون بعض الأعراض أشد من بعض هذا اجتماع الأمثال تلك الأعراض في محل واحد ، والذين يقولون باستحالة جمع المثلين ربما عدوها في المتضادين وحينئذ لا يكون قسمة المختافين إلى المتضادين وغير المتضادين قسمة عام إلى خاصين ، لأن المثلين أيضاً يدخلان في المتضادين ، وحينئذ ينبغي أن يقسم الغيران إلى المتضادين والمختلفين والمتضادين إلى المثلين وإلى غيرهما .
Question: It is impossible to combine two similars according to us and the philosophers, contrary to the Mu'tazila. We argue: assuming combination, no distinction occurs by essences and necessary concomitants, otherwise they would not be similars; nor by accidents, because the relation of an accident to each of them is equal, so it is not more appropriate for it to be an accident of one than of the other, for all accidents. So it would be a cause for each of them, and then no distinction remains at all, so the two become one, which is impossible. The opponent objected: the ruling of a thing is the ruling of its similar, so if the essence is receptive to the other. The answer: combination necessitates turning the two into one. (2) I say: Lack of distinction does not indicate union; rather, it at most indicates lack of knowledge of the distinct. And the judgment that two combined similars are not distinguished by accidents is refuted by the convergence of lines that when combined become a single point in position, yet they are distinct lines and being so is among their accidents. And the judgment that combination necessitates turning the two into one is a bare claim without demonstration. And some Mu'tazila allowed the combination of similars and said that the cause for some accidents being stronger than others is the combination of those accidents in one locus. And those who say it is impossible to combine similars may count them as opposites; then the division of different things into opposites and non-opposites is not a division of a general into two specifics, because similars also enter into opposites. Then one should divide two things into opposites and different, and opposites into similars and others.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage states the thesis that combining two similars is impossible, adhering to the Ash'ari and philosopher position against the Mu'tazila. Proof: if two similars combined, there would be no distinction by essence or accident, so they would become one, impossible. The opponent objects with a syllogism about ruling of a thing applying to its similar, but the response is that combination annihilates duality. The commentator then criticizes: lack of distinction does not prove union, only ignorance of distinction. He provides a counterexample: lines that converge at a point are distinct yet occupy the same position. He also notes that some Mu'tazila allow combining similars by explaining gradation of accidents via combination. Finally, he points out a logical confusion: those who deny combination of similars often treat them as opposites, which would change the traditional classification of two things.

Key Terms
مثلان
جمع
امتياز
عرض
اتحاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If two things cannot be distinguished, they must be identical.
Correction: The commentator clarifies that lack of distinction may simply reflect our ignorance of the real differences, not numerical identity.

Checkpoint
What counterexample does the commentator give to refute the claim that combined similars cannot be distinguished by accidents?
A) Two humans sharing humanity
B) Lines converging at a single point
C) Blackness and whiteness in the same subject
D) God's attributes being neither same nor different

Key Takeaway
The debate over combining similars hinges on whether lack of distinction implies identity, which the commentator denies using the example of convergent lines.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself unable to tell two things apart, do you ever assume they are the same without further investigation? How might that habit affect your understanding of Allah's names and attributes? What is one step you will take this week to examine an assumption you hold about the nature of Allah?
?
This lesson shows that even scholars can mistake a lack of knowledge for a proof of identity. Where in your own life have you been content with a shallow understanding of a deep theological point, conflating ignorance with certainty? What specific question about your faith will you not stop asking until you have a well-grounded answer?
?
The commentator warns against bare claims without demonstration. Have you ever accepted a theological argument simply because it sounded convincing or came from an authority, without testing its logical structure? Commit today to identify one argument you have accepted uncritically and to study its counterarguments with the same seriousness.

The Commentator's Note on Razi's Omitted Opponents
Commentator notes Razi's list is incomplete.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the opposition to Razi's position is broader than the groups he explicitly lists, as noted by the commentator.

Core Idea
Razi's list of opponents is not exhaustive.

The commentator inserts a brief observation on the incompleteness of Imam Razi's enumeration of groups that deny God's universal power. While Razi mentions three categories, the commentator reminds us that many other sects share similar views, making the opposition wider than Razi's list suggests. This note highlights the importance of recognizing the full scope of theological disagreement.

Guided Path
1
Read the commentator's note
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice that the commentator is pointing out an omission in Razi's list.
The passage is a single sentence, but it contains an important qualification.
2
Identify the groups mentioned
The commentator says Razi mentioned only three groups: the philosophers (falasifa), the dualists (thanawiyya), and a few from the Mu'tazila (like al-Nazzam). List them clearly.
These are the explicit groups in Razi's original discussion.
3
Understand the commentator's point
The commentator states that 'all the sects are not confined to these,' meaning there are many other groups that hold similar views. This broadens the scope of opposition.
This is a textual note, not a doctrinal argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقول : لم يذكر من المخالفين غير الفلاسفة والثنوية وقوما معدودين من المعتزله ، وليس جميع الفرق محصورين في هؤلاء .
I say: He did not mention from among the opponents except the philosophers, the dualists, and a counted few from the Mu'tazila. And all the sects are not confined to these.
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This short passage is a note from the commentator observing that Imam Razi's list of groups who deny God's power over all things is incomplete. Razi mentioned only the philosophers (falasifa), the dualists (thanawiyya), and a few from the Mu'tazila (like al-Nazzam). The commentator points out that there are many other sects holding similar views that are not limited to these. This serves as a reminder that the opposition is broader.

Key Terms

الفلاسفة
الثنوية
المعتزلة
فرق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that Razi's list of opponents is complete.
Correction: The commentator clarifies that the list is not exhaustive; many other sects hold contrary views.

Checkpoint
What does the commentator say about Razi's list of opponents?
A) It is complete.
B) It omitted the philosophers.
C) It omitted many groups and is not exhaustive.
D) It included the Mu'tazila entirely.

Key Takeaway
Razi's list of opposing groups is incomplete; the commentator broadens the scope of opposition beyond the mentioned groups.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a list of opposing views from a respected scholar, do you stop investigating further? How does this commentator's correction reveal a hidden assumption in your own learning? What will you do to ensure you seek a broader understanding?
?
Have you ever dismissed an entire group of people because you only knew a few of their arguments? In light of this lesson, what step will you take to examine your own intellectual blind spots?
?
If even Imam Razi's enumeration of opponents was incomplete, how much more incomplete might your own understanding be? Will you commit to actively seeking out and engaging with the full range of opposing views, even if it challenges your comfort?

Sects and Disagreements
intermediate
8 min
Refuting the Philosophers and Dualists on God's Power Over Evil
Imam Razi refutes two objections to God's power over evil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two key objections raised by philosophers and dualists against God's power to create evil, and the initial rebuttal offered by Imam Razi.

Core Idea
Evil does not diminish God's power; objections stem from flawed assumptions about agency.

Imam Razi here tackles two separate groups who doubted God's ability to create evil. The first is the philosophers who argued that from a single First Principle only one effect can arise, ruling out multiple creations including evil. The second is the dualists and Magians who claimed a good agent cannot produce evil. Razi's response challenges the very premise of their argument, forcing them to clarify their definitions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then its translation. Notice the two distinct objections and Razi's brief answer.
The passage is dense; let the author's rhythm come through first.
2
Identify the two objections
Find where the philosophers' objection is stated and where the dualists' objection begins. Note the key terms for each group.
Philosophers: 'الفلاسفة'; Dualists: 'الثنوية والمجوس'.
3
Understand the first objection and its prior answer
The philosophers' argument is that from the One only one can proceed. Razi says the answer has 'preceded' — meaning it was given earlier in the text. Reflect on why this objection matters for the issue of evil.
The prior answer is not repeated here, but the implication is that the One can produce multiple things through mediation.
4
Analyze the second objection
The dualists claim that a good agent cannot do evil because the doer of good is good and the doer of evil is evil, and one agent cannot be both. Notice the underlying assumption: that actions define the agent's nature.
This is a common theological problem: how can a good God create evil?
5
Examine Razi's terse answer as a logical challenge
Razi asks: if by 'good' and 'evil' you mean the one who produces good and evil, then why claim a single agent cannot do both? If you mean something else (e.g., the agent's own nature), then clarify. This shifts the burden to the objector to define terms consistently.
Razi's answer is a logical dismantling of the dualists' premise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الفلاسفة فقد منعوا أن يصدر عن الواحد أكثر من واحد وقد تقدم الجواب عن حجتهم . وأما الثنوية والمجوس زعموا أنه غير قادر على الشر لأن فاعل الخيرات خير وفاعل الشرور شرير، و الفاعل الواحد يستحيل أن يكون خيرا شريراً . الجواب : إن عنيت الخ.. والشرير موجد الخير والشر فلم قلت إن الفاعل الواحد يستحيل أن يكون كذلك وإن عنيت غيره فبينوا
As for the philosophers, they prevented that from the One more than one should issue, and the answer to their argument has preceded. As for the dualists and the Magians, they claimed that He is not powerful over evil, because the doer of good deeds is good and the doer of evils is evil, and it is impossible for a single doer to be good and evil. The answer: If you mean [the good and evil] and the evil is the one who brings about good and evil, then why did you say that a single doer cannot be that? And if you mean something else, then explain.
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage presents two objections and a brief answer. First, the philosophers (falasifa) argue that from the One (the First Principle) only one effect can proceed. Razi says the answer to their argument has already been given (presumably earlier). Second, the dualists (thanawiyya) and Magians (majoos) claim that God cannot create evil because the agent of good is good, and the agent of evil is evil, and one and the same agent cannot be both good and evil. Razi's answer is a challenge: if by 'good' and 'evil' you mean the one who produces good and evil, then why do you assert that a single agent cannot do both? If you mean something else (e.g., the agent's own nature), then explain. This answer is elliptical; the commentator later provides a fuller explanation.

Key Terms
الفلاسفة
الثنوية
المجوس
خير
شر
فاعل واحد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A good agent cannot produce evil because its nature determines its acts; therefore God cannot create evil.
Correction: Razi shows that the objection confuses the agent's moral character with the actions it performs. God, as the sole creator, creates both good and evil in terms of outcome, while His essence remains unchanged.

Checkpoint
What is the dualists' main argument against God's ability to create evil?
A) Evil is impossible to create.
B) A good agent cannot create evil because the agent's nature determines its acts.
C) God lacks power over evil.
D) Evil requires a separate evil creator.

Key Takeaway
Razi's response shifts the burden to the objector to clarify whether they are defining agency by actions or essence, thus exposing a flaw in the dualists' reasoning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever questioned God's goodness because of the evil you see or experience? How does Razi's challenge to define your terms force you to reexamine your assumptions about God and evil?
?
When you witness evil, do you unconsciously assume that the agent of that evil must be evil in essence? How does this lesson challenge that assumption regarding God?
?
If you truly believe that God is the sole creator of all things, including evil, how does that affect your response to personal suffering? What does it demand of you in your relationship with Him?

Sects and Disagreements

4 min
Dualist Doctrines on Good and Evil
Commentator enumerates dualist sects and their shared premise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the beliefs of three dualist groups about the sources of good and evil.

Core Idea
Dualists claim good and evil require separate creators because they are opposites.

Before we can understand the refutation, we must first hear what the dualists actually believed. The commentator introduces three groups—the Magians, Manichaeans, and Dahriyya—and their common argument that a single agent cannot be both good and evil. Let's see their claims.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on dualist doctrines
Read the Arabic and translation carefully to see which groups are mentioned and what each group says about the creators of good and evil.
2
Identify the three groups and their views
List the Magians, Manichaeans, and Dahriyya and note their specific creators: Yazdan/Ahriman, light/darkness, and similar.
The Dahriyya are materialists who hold a similar dualism.
3
Understand the common argument
Notice that all groups agree: a good being does only good, an evil being does only evil, so it's impossible for one being to do both.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقول : المجوس من الثنوية يقولون إن فاعل الخير ( يزدان ، وفاعل الشر « أهر من ، ويعنون بهما ماكما وشيطانا والله تعالى منزه عن فعل الخير والشر ، والمانوية يقولون إن فاعلهما النور والظلمة ، والديحانية يذهبون إلى مثل ذلك ، والجميع يقولون بأن الخير هو الذى يكون جميع أفعاله خيرا والشرير هو الذي يكون أفعاله شراً ، ومحال أن يكون فاعلهما واحدا وجوابهم أن الخير والشر لا يكونان لذاتهما خيراً وشرا بل بالإضافة إلى غيرهما ، وإذا أمكن أن يكون شيء واحد بالقياس إلى واحد خيرا وبالقياس إلى غيره شرا أمكن أن يكون فاعل ذلك الشيء واحدا .
I say: The Magians from the dualists say that the doer of good is Yazdan, and the doer of evil is Ahriman, and by them they mean [something like] Maka and Shaitan, and God Almighty is exalted above doing good and evil. The Manichaeans say that their doer is light and darkness. The Dahriyya go to something like that. And all of them say that the good one is he whose all deeds are good, and the evil one is he whose deeds are evil, and it is impossible for their doer to be one. Their answer is that good and evil are not good and evil in their essences, but rather by relation to others. And when it is possible that one thing is good in relation to one and evil in relation to another, it is possible that the doer of that thing be one.
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage is the commentator's explanation of the dualist sects and the correct answer to them. He mentions three groups: the Magians (Majoos) who believe in two separate creators, Yazdan (good) and Ahriman (evil); the Manichaeans (Maanawiyya) who attribute good and evil to light and darkness; and the Dahriyya (materialists) who hold similar views. All claim that a single agent cannot be both good and evil. The commentator provides the refutation: good and evil are not intrinsic qualities but are relative (bil idaafa). A single action can be good for one person and evil for another. Therefore, a single agent can produce both good and evil, as the same act can have different relations. Thus, the dualists' argument that two agents are necessary fails.

Key Terms
المجوس
الثنوية
المانوية
الديحانية
يزدان
أهرمن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what do the Magians believe about who does good and evil?
A) God does good and evil.
B) Yazdan does good, Ahriman does evil.
C) Light does good, darkness does evil.
D) There is no doer of good or evil.

Key Takeaway
Three dualist groups—Magians, Manichaeans, and Dahriyya—all argue that good and evil require separate agents because a single being cannot be the source of both.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience evil or suffering in your life, do you ever feel that it cannot possibly come from the same God who gives you good? In what ways does this lesson expose the danger of dividing your trust between two sources?
?
The dualists claimed that a good being can only do good. Look at your own actions: are you willing to admit that your own good deeds are mixed with selfishness, and your failures with moments of goodness? If you are a mixture yourself, how can you insist that the Creator must be untainted by evil?
?
If you truly believe that Allah alone is the source of all that happens—both the good you celebrate and the trials you endure—what would need to change in how you respond to hardship tomorrow? Will you commit to that?
